The authors analyze *Her Story*, *Return of the Obra Dinn*, and *Subserial Network* as examples. *Her Story* requires players to build search terms in a database of interview clips and learn through trial and error when keywords retrieve useful evidence. *Return of the Obra Dinn* asks players to infer identities and causes of death from incomplete evidence. *Subserial Network* asks players to identify suspect communities by applying decision rules to messages, links, and online traces.

For the larger GBLS project, this source is valuable because it treats commercial games as adjacent simulations for library professional judgment rather than as library-skills games in a narrow sense. The authors do not claim that playing *Her Story* is the same as conducting a reference interview. Instead, they suggest that investigative games can give students supplemental practice with the reasoning habits that reference work requires: abductive inference about tacit needs, deductive source selection, inductive search revision, rule testing, metacognitive reflection, and perseverance through ambiguity. This is especially useful for thinking about GBLS in LIS education, staff training, and professional development.

The article should be cited as conceptual rather than evaluative evidence. The authors do not report a classroom implementation or assess whether students who play investigative games become better reference workers. They also identify limitations: instructors need student buy-in, the distinction between reasoning types may need explicit scaffolding, some mystery games include violent or upsetting content, and games rooted in dominant-culture tropes may not support critical information literacy without careful selection and reflection. Still, the article gives the review a strong vocabulary for one branch of GBLS instruction: using games not merely to teach facts about library tools, but to cultivate the judgment, metacognition, and interpretive flexibility required for library service.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after Seay's "A Liminal Response to Watching Paint Dry" paragraph.

Suggested text: Burgess and Wallace (2021) shift game-based instruction from library orientation to reference education. Using concept analysis, they argue that reference workers need clinical intuition and metacognition to adapt interviews, resource selection, and search construction in real time, and that investigative video games can supplement MLIS reference teaching by letting students practice abductive, deductive, and inductive reasoning through rule testing and immediate feedback in games such as *Her Story*, *Return of the Obra Dinn*, and *Subserial Network*. The article is conceptual rather than outcome evidence, but it gives GBLS a vocabulary for using commercial games as adjacent simulations for professional judgment, especially in online or asynchronous LIS courses.

# Miller, R. E. (2019). Libraries may teach some skills through mobile application games. *Evidence Based Library and Information Practice, 14*(3), 150-152. https://doi.org/10.18438/eblip29587

Unique identifier: `millerLibrariesMayTeach2019`; Zotero item key `TWRZ3LTD`.

Miller (2019) is an evidence summary and critical appraisal of Kaneko, Saito, Nohara, Kudo, and Yamada's (2018) study of "Library Adventures: Unveil the Hidden Mysteries!," a mobile application game for university library instruction at Kyushu University in Fukuoka, Japan. The reviewed study compared a game-based learning environment with a web-based tutorial for first-year undergraduate students. Thirty-six students began the experiment, 32 completed it, and participants were divided into experimental and control groups. Both groups received instruction on local library procedures, including library hours, collection arrangement, and methods for locating books and articles. The control group used a web tutorial, while the experimental group used a mobile game that required students to move through the physical library and complete location-based tasks. The design included a pretest, learning experience, post-test, motivation questionnaire, and a one-month follow-up with declarative knowledge and behavioral tests.

The evidence summary is especially useful because it distinguishes several kinds of outcomes that are often blurred in library game reports. Miller reports that the game and tutorial groups gained similar levels of declarative knowledge, and that both groups lost some knowledge after one month. The mobile-game group, however, performed better on some embodied library tasks, such as borrowing a journal and locating a newspaper article, while tutorial users completed study-room reservation more quickly. The strongest reported difference was motivational: students in the game condition scored higher on attention, relevance, and satisfaction in the Instructional Materials Motivation Survey, which draws on Keller's ARCS model of Attention, Relevance, Confidence, and Satisfaction. Confidence did not differ between groups.

Miller's commentary emphasizes why the Kaneko et al. study matters for library instruction: it directly compares a game and a tutorial rather than only describing a single game intervention, and the game requires physical movement through the library rather than treating mobile delivery as a purely screen-based experience. In GBLS terms, "Library Adventures" is not simply a digital object; it is a hybrid service format connecting mobile prompts, physical navigation, local procedures, and task performance. The article therefore supports a view of game-based library instruction as situated practice. A game may not produce superior quiz scores, but it may better support learners in connecting procedural knowledge to embodied library use.

At the same time, Miller's appraisal cautions against overclaiming. The original study's recruitment, consent, inclusion criteria, and reporting are not fully described, and the relationship between game-stage analytics, pre/post-test outcomes, and motivation measures remains underdeveloped. For the larger GBLS project, this makes the source valuable less as proof that mobile games "work" and more as a compact example of what stronger GBLS evaluation might include: comparison groups, delayed follow-up, behavioral tasks, validated motivation instruments, and attention to where players succeed or struggle inside the game. It also reinforces that assessment should separate knowledge retention, task performance, motivation, confidence, and transfer rather than collapsing them into attendance or enjoyment.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Broussard (2014) paragraph. Proposed text: "Miller's (2019) evidence summary of Kaneko et al.'s mobile *Library Adventure* study reinforces this assessment lesson. The reviewed experiment found similar declarative-knowledge gains for game and tutorial users, mixed behavioral performance after one month, and stronger game-group motivation on attention, relevance, and satisfaction; for GBLS, the value lies in separating motivation, retention, task performance, confidence, and transfer instead of treating game enjoyment as an outcome by itself."

# **Lee, J. H., Cho, H., Fox, V., & Perti, A. (2013). User-centered approach in creating a metadata schema for video games and interactive media. In *Proceedings of the 13th ACM/IEEE-CS Joint Conference on Digital Libraries* (pp. 229-238). ACM. https://doi.org/10.1145/2467696.2467702**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `XSJKWBVW`; Better BibTeX citation key `leeUsercenteredApproachCreating2013`; attachment key `ZX5BQVDA`.

Lee, Cho, Fox, and Perti present a user-centered metadata schema for video games and interactive media, developed through a collaboration between the University of Washington Information School and the Seattle Interactive Media Museum. The article begins from a problem that is central to game-based library services: games are increasingly part of cultural, archival, museum, and digital-library collections, but existing organizational systems often describe them with vague, inconsistent, or book-centered metadata. Traditional library standards such as FRBR, RDA, and LCSH tend to capture basic bibliographic information such as title, platform, publisher, and format, but they do not fully represent how games are played, experienced, discovered, or compared.

The project's goal was to create a standardized metadata schema and encoding scheme that could describe video games and interactive media across organizations and domains. The authors emphasize that a game schema must serve many stakeholders, including players, parents, collectors, academics, developers, curators, librarians, and preservation institutions. This matters for GBLS because a library game collection is not only a shelf or circulation category. It depends on discovery, advisory, preservation, access, and meaning-making systems that match how patrons actually seek and evaluate games.

The study combines domain analysis, in-depth interviews, user-centered design, personas, facet analysis, and cataloging tests. In Phase I, the team collected 61 metadata elements from game websites, WorldCat, Amazon, and other organizational systems, then evaluated them through personas such as a player, parent, collector, academic, game designer, and curator/librarian. That work produced CORE16, a set of 16 core elements. In Phase II, the team expanded the master list to 135 candidate metadata elements by examining more game-related websites and analyzing genre terms. The authors then interviewed 24 gamers, ranging from casual to hardcore, to understand game-related information needs and seeking behaviors. Based on those interviews and their cost-benefit, objectivity, stability, source, and ease-of-acquisition criteria, they proposed a 46-element recommended set.

The interview findings show why ordinary bibliographic description is not enough. Users wanted subject access points that distinguished gameplay genre from theme, setting, plot/narrative, mood, style, and affect. They recognized that genre alone can be overloaded or misleading because the same genre label may contain very different play experiences. Visual information was also highly valued: box art, screenshots, trailers, gameplay videos, characters, visual style, and artistic direction helped users decide whether a game matched their tastes. Users cared about companies and franchises, especially developers and publishers, because production teams and franchises often function as recognizable markers of quality, style, and expected experience.

The article also shows that game information needs change by stage. Some users seek promotional descriptions, images, and videos after announcement; release dates and region information before purchase; screenshots and gameplay videos at the time of purchase; walkthroughs or wikis during play; and similar games after completing a satisfying game. The authors note that this creates a spoiler problem: metadata elements such as plot/narrative, theme, or character information may be valuable for discovery but can also reduce playability if too much is revealed. This is a useful GBLS point because library discovery systems need to support both finding and experiencing games without flattening them into overdescribed records.

The recommended metadata set includes traditional descriptive elements as well as game-specific ones: title, alternative title, edition, format, series, franchise/universe, platform, developer, publisher, distributor, special hardware, online capabilities, system requirements, credits, official website, price, retail release date, controls, packaging, number of players, rating, purpose, customization options, difficulty levels, achievements/awards/trophies, region, language, identifier, box art/cover, screenshots, trailers, gameplay videos, genre/gameplay, style, plot/narrative, theme, setting, mood/affect, temporal aspect, presentation, point of view, character names, character types, links to historical events, type of ending, and visual style. The breadth of this list makes visible how much game discovery depends on audiovisual, experiential, technical, social, and narrative attributes.

For the larger GBLS project, Lee et al. provide an important infrastructure source. Their schema supports circulation, collection development, advisory, preservation, curriculum support, and cultural heritage work by showing what must be described for games to be findable and meaningful. It also foregrounds the practical labor involved: catalogers need sources of information beyond the game box, controlled vocabularies for elements such as visual style and mood, decisions about manifestation-level versus item-level description, strategies for user-generated data versus authoritative accuracy, and enough domain knowledge to describe rapidly changing genres, platforms, online capabilities, and play forms.

The article's limitations are also useful. The authors do not claim the schema is complete, and they present it as a starting point for community iteration. The interview sample was small and heavily male, and the schema required further systematic evaluation through application to sample game collections. Still, it is one of the strongest GBLS sources for arguing that game services require metadata designed around actual user behavior. Libraries that collect or promote games need discovery systems that reflect how patrons search for games, compare them, avoid unwanted content, locate compatible hardware, find similar experiences, and understand games as cultural artifacts rather than generic software objects.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the de Groat (2015) paragraph.

Suggested text: Lee, Cho, Fox, and Perti (2013) make the user warrant for game metadata explicit. Working with the Seattle Interactive Media Museum, they combined domain analysis of existing catalog and game-site elements, personas, cataloging tests, facet analysis, and interviews with 24 gamers to propose CORE16 and a 46-element recommended schema. The schema adds game-specific access points such as gameplay/genre, style, plot/narrative, theme, setting, mood/affect, visual style, point of view, online capabilities, special hardware, and distribution data; for GBLS, it shows that discovery must support how players, parents, collectors, educators, scholars, developers, and librarians actually search while managing source-of-information, spoiler, stability, authority, and cataloger-expertise problems.

# Guo, Y. R., & Goh, D. H.-L. (2016). Library Escape: User-centered design of an information literacy game. *The Library Quarterly: Information, Community, Policy, 86*(3), 330-355.

Unique identifier: `guoLibraryEscapeUserCentered2016`; Zotero item key `M4BHEU53`; attachment key `BWVK8REL`.

Guo and Goh (2016) describe the user-centered design of *Library Escape*, a digital game intended to teach university students information literacy (IL). The article begins from a problem familiar to academic libraries: students need to seek, locate, navigate, evaluate, and synthesize information, but many are reluctant to initiate help-seeking with librarians and often prefer search-engine results of uncertain quality. Digital game-based learning is presented as a possible response because games can support engagement, problem solving, feedback, immersion, and active information seeking. The authors also caution that educational games often fail when designers assume learning content can simply be attached to entertaining mechanics without involving the people who will actually use the game.

The study's main contribution is methodological. Guo and Goh use two user-centered approaches: a participatory design workshop and a subsequent user evaluation. The design was guided by Kuhlthau's Information Search Process (ISP) Model, whose six stages became the organizing structure for the game: task initiation, topic selection, prefocus exploration, focus formulation, information collection, and search closure. Seven university students participated in the initial workshop after playing existing IL games and discussing what they liked and disliked. They collaboratively developed a low-fidelity prototype through focus-group discussion and storyboarding. Ten university students then participated in individual interviews to evaluate the low-fidelity prototype, producing feedback that the authors used to revise the game into a high-fidelity prototype.

The low-fidelity version imagined *Library Escape* as a role-playing adventure. The protagonist, Tom, receives a poor IL grade and is pushed into a deserted library where a librarian, Ms. Senka, helps him complete six missions. Each mission maps to a stage of the ISP Model and takes place in a different library venue, including closed stacks, the reference desk, open shelves, a digital library, a cafe, and an exhibition area. Quests introduce IL definitions, library anxiety, information-seeking models, the ISP Model, applications of the ISP Model, and broad questions about objectivity, information need, and search closure. This version embedded IL content in hidden objects and used mission completion, scores, certificates, leaderboards, status bars, and social sharing as game elements.

The user evaluation changed the design substantially. Participants found some elements boring, too obvious, too philosophical, insufficiently practical, or disconnected from what they expected to learn. The revised high-fidelity prototype changed the backstory from a video to comic strips, made the librarian a male ghost guardian named Mr. Senka, added a magic-wand rescue plot, simplified the final certificate, renamed missions after ISP stages, adjusted venues, and reduced the number of quests per mission from four to three so the game could go deeper rather than broader. The revised learning content became more practical: topic selection, types of scientific literature, library anxiety, scholarly publication cycles, Google Scholar, OPAC use, building blocks, pearl growing, successive fractions, evaluation of retrieved articles, reflection on the ISP Model, authorship responsibilities, plagiarism, and citations. The game also added explorer, bonus, and gateway objects to avoid linear clicking and encourage exploration.

For the GBLS project, the article is valuable because it makes game-based library instruction look like an iterative service-design problem rather than a finished novelty object. Guo and Goh identify three design tensions that matter across GBLS. First, designers should be accommodating yet pragmatic: user input is necessary, but users may disagree with each other and may not understand IL pedagogy. Second, designers must balance enjoyment and learning: too much content makes a game feel mechanical, while too much entertainment can displace educational purpose. Third, designers should be guided yet flexible: a theoretical framework can sequence learning, but players still need room to explore, choose, and feel control inside missions.

The article should not be used as evidence that *Library Escape* improved IL outcomes, because it reports prototype design rather than a playable implementation with learning assessment. The authors explicitly note that future work should include observations, game-activity log analysis, expert inspection by educators and game designers, and further evaluation after implementation. Still, the source gives the review a strong example of participatory, theoretically grounded GBLS design. It supports the argument that library games should be built with users, tested early, revised materially, and connected to recognizable learning models while preserving the agency and suspense that make games worth playing.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Jerrett, Bothma, and de Beer (2017) paragraph. Proposed text: "Guo and Goh (2016) add a design-process perspective through *Library Escape*, a prototype IL game structured around Kuhlthau's Information Search Process model. Their participatory design workshop and user-evaluation interviews show how an academic-library game can translate task initiation, topic selection, prefocus exploration, focus formulation, information collection, and search closure into missions, quests, venues, characters, hidden objects, rewards, and reflection prompts while remaining open to substantial user-driven redesign."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Design for Reflection and Transfer`, after the Jerrett et al. (2017) paragraph. Proposed text: "Guo and Goh (2016) also clarify that reflection and transfer have to be designed through iteration. Their *Library Escape* revisions moved from broad, sometimes abstract IL content toward more practical tasks such as Google Scholar, OPAC use, search strategies, evaluating retrieved articles, authorship responsibilities, plagiarism, and citations, while keeping the ISP model as an underlying scaffold; the lesson for GBLS is to let user feedback reshape story, pacing, difficulty, and task design without losing the learning framework."

# **Chapman, A. (2021). Trials of metadata: Emerging schemas for videogame cataloguing. *Journal of Library Metadata, 21*(3-4), 63-103. https://doi.org/10.1080/19386389.2021.2007729**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `58K7Q6BM`; Better BibTeX citation key `chapmanTrialsMetadataEmerging2021`; attachment key `AAMWMYVE`.

Chapman examines emerging videogame metadata schemas at a moment when games are increasingly entering museums, archives, schools, and libraries, but widely accepted cataloging guidance remains unsettled. The article is valuable for GBLS because it compares schemas created by information professionals, fan communities, and commercial platforms rather than treating library cataloging in isolation. Chapman asks how videogames challenge and expand traditional metadata standards, especially Resource Description and Access (RDA), Library of Congress vocabularies, and genre/form practice.

The literature review situates videogame cataloging between two older problem areas: three-dimensional objects and electronic resources. Physical games and peripherals can resemble realia, toys, or board games; digital games can resemble software, machine-readable data files, or networked electronic resources. Yet videogames often combine both. A cartridge, disk, manual, controller, accessory, online dependency, patch, downloadable add-on, and executable code may all matter. Chapman uses examples such as *Stack-Up*, *Colossal Cave Adventure*, *Undertale*, *Doom*, and *The Legend of Zelda: Ocarina of Time/Master Quest* to show how titles, editions, versions, platforms, carriers, add-ons, and sources of information become difficult in game cataloging.

The method is comparative analysis. Chapman analyzes five schemas or schema-like systems: the Core Metadata Schema for Cataloguing Video Games (CMS) developed by GAMECIP; the University of Washington GAMER Group's Video Game Metadata Schema (VGMS); MobyGames; Steam; and Itch.io. The first two are academic/library-oriented schemas. MobyGames represents a long-running fan/community database with archival ambitions and documented standards. Steam and Itch.io represent commercial digital distribution platforms whose descriptive elements and user-generated tags reveal what game marketplaces treat as useful for discovery. Chapman compares these systems to core RDA elements as interpreted by OLAC's *Best Practices for Cataloguing Video Games Using RDA and MARC21*, and then analyzes their treatment of genre.

The findings show both compatibility and tension with RDA. Many videogame elements can be crosswalked to RDA, but games require more medium-specific granularity. Title can be difficult because packaging, disk labels, menus, title screens, regional releases, alternate titles, abbreviations, colloquial names, and series/franchise naming may conflict. Statements of responsibility do not map cleanly because games are produced by teams, studios, publishers, distributors, voice actors, testers, and other contributors rather than a single authorial figure. Publication is complicated by retail release dates, region, copyright dates, digital updates, developer self-publishing, and changing company names. Edition and version are complicated by commercial editions, software versions, patches, and downloadable content.

Chapman's analysis strongly supports platform as a distinct metadata element. RDA and OLAC can treat platform information through edition and system details, but all of the videogame schemas provide a platform element because platform can define compatibility, gameplay, historical context, and expression-level difference. The article also highlights the need for system requirements, developer, publisher, summary or game description, media format, release/update information, and carrier descriptions that are more precise than generic computer-carrier terms. For GBLS, this matters because patrons and staff need to know not only what a game is called but whether it can be played, on what hardware, with what dependencies, and in what version.

The genre discussion is especially important. Chapman shows that Library of Congress subject headings and genre/form vocabularies remain thin for games, while game websites and fan databases use genre as a major discovery facet. The article separates user-generated genres, narrative genres, visual styles, and gameplay genres. Steam and Itch.io tags can be rich but subjective and unstable. MobyGames and VGMS provide more structured vocabularies that distinguish gameplay from narrative theme, setting, visual presentation, art style, perspective, mood, and related facets. Chapman's main point is that game genre is overloaded: a single label may mix mechanics, narrative, visual style, audience expectations, marketing terms, and player culture. Faceted vocabularies help reduce that overload.

For the larger GBLS project, Chapman extends the metadata strand by showing that game cataloging is both interoperable and distinctive. Libraries can use RDA and MARC structures, but games require enhanced description if catalogs are to support scholarly research, preservation, circulation, discovery, and player advisory. The article also usefully draws attention to gaps. Because many schemas focus on manifestations or expressions, they may underdescribe material-culture elements such as peripheral hardware, manuals, packaging, sound, video, digital file characteristics, color, number of players, connectivity, and ratings. Those details are not decorative; they affect access, compatibility, preservation, and patron decision-making.

Chapman should be used as a synthesis and comparison source rather than as a user study. Its value lies in clarifying how several schema communities are solving similar problems and where standards still need development. For GBLS, the takeaway is practical: game collections need metadata that can communicate across library systems while remaining faithful to games as interactive, technical, visual, narrative, commercial, and community-described objects. That work is ongoing because game genres, platforms, distribution models, and user vocabularies keep changing.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Lee, Cho, Fox, and Perti (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Chapman (2021) extends this schema discussion by comparing GAMECIP's CMS, the GAMER Group's VGMS, MobyGames, Steam, and Itch.io against OLAC/RDA elements and genre treatment. She finds that emerging game schemas converge on medium-specific elements that RDA treats awkwardly or does not make core: platform distinct from edition and system requirements, equal attention to developer and publisher, granular media and platform vocabularies, release/update/version/additional-content data, and multifaceted genre split across narrative, visual, and gameplay facets. For GBLS, her analysis shows that game cataloging can remain crosswalkable to RDA while still needing enhanced description for scholarly access, player discovery, and legacy software preservation.

# Nance, M. K. (2022). Playing for keeps: How academic libraries are prioritizing student mental health and well-being through play. *Journal of Play in Adulthood, 4*(2), 163-176.

Unique identifier: `nancePlayingKeepsHow2022`; Zotero item key `BUW225C6`; attachment key `RSWIGWEQ`.

Nance (2022) argues that academic libraries have become active contributors to student mental health and well-being by providing play-based materials, spaces, and programs. The article is a narrative synthesis and professional rationale rather than a new empirical evaluation, but it is directly relevant to Game Based Library Services because it reframes play as part of holistic academic support. Nance begins from the claim that higher education has historically separated academic success from mental health support, assigning the former to teaching faculty, libraries, and academic-support units and the latter to counseling, health, residential life, and student life. Rising levels of student anxiety, depression, and stress make that separation less tenable. Because academic libraries are centrally located, open for long hours, already trusted as student gathering spaces, and tied to reliable information, they are positioned to normalize low-barrier wellness support.

The article's core move is to connect adult playfulness to student coping, academic success, and healthy breaks. Nance summarizes research suggesting that play and playfulness in adulthood can increase engagement, creativity, mindfulness, innovation, social skills, coping, and cognitive performance while decreasing boredom, stress, tension, depression, aggression, and anxiety. She also links student mental health to academic performance and retention, arguing that libraries' academic mission now requires attention to emotional wellness. Play-based library services are not substitutes for counseling or therapy, but their accessibility and nonclinical setting may reduce stigma, support self-reflection, and help students practice balance, mindfulness, and stress relief.

Nance identifies several practical service forms. First, academic libraries can support micro-breaks: short interruptions from work or study that help restore focus, reduce fatigue, and maintain engagement. Coloring pages, Legos, Play-Doh, kinetic sand, puzzles, video games, and similar materials give students a structured way to step away from intensive study while keeping their attention grounded in an unrelated task. Second, coloring initiatives are presented as inexpensive, customizable, social, and meditative activities. Adult coloring books, mandalas, geometric images, and coloring stations offer enough structure to avoid anxiety about creating from scratch while still allowing choice, control, and mindfulness.

Third, Nance discusses Legos and building materials as kinetic resources for stress relief, creative thinking, collaboration, and feelings of accomplishment. She notes examples such as a Lego playing station at Rutgers' Art Library, Princeton's Build and Play section in a library makerspace, and Columbia's in-library-use Lego build kits. These materials matter because they create shared, tactile spaces where students can make something, focus away from coursework, and return to study with a different emotional state. Fourth, Nance argues that video games can support psychological and physiological stress relief by offering immersion, distraction, autonomy, competence, relatedness, and social play. Circulating consoles, providing designated gaming spaces, and offering casual gameplay can reach students who may not be drawn to more conventional wellness activities.

For the GBLS project, the article is useful because it widens the definition of game-based library services beyond instruction, collections, or entertainment. It treats games and playful materials as care infrastructure. A coloring station, Lego kit, video game console, therapy-animal event, puzzle table, scavenger hunt, escape room, or library mini-golf program can become part of a broader wellness ecology when it gives students permission to take breaks, socialize, regulate stress, and choose healthier coping practices. This is especially important for academic libraries, where prolonged study, finals culture, and stigma around breaks can produce an unhealthy model of dedication.

The limitations are important. Nance does not evaluate a particular library intervention or provide outcome data from students using play-based wellness services. Some supporting evidence comes from broader play, art therapy, adult playfulness, counseling, and gaming research rather than from library-specific assessment. Still, the article gives the literature review a needed bridge between wellness research and concrete academic-library practice. It supports a GBLS agenda in which libraries evaluate not only attendance and satisfaction, but also stress relief, belonging, healthy coping, access to wellness resources, normalization of breaks, and the relationship between playful library spaces and student academic persistence.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Emerging Area: Wellness, Mental Health, and Care`, after the Brennan (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Nance (2022) extends the wellness thread to academic libraries by framing play as holistic student-support infrastructure. Coloring stations, Legos, Play-Doh, kinetic sand, puzzles, video games, therapy-animal events, scavenger hunts, escape rooms, and library mini-golf can function as low-barrier micro-breaks that normalize taking a break, reduce stigma around help-seeking, support mindfulness and healthy coping, and connect mental well-being to academic persistence."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## Circulating and In-Library Collections`, after the Slobuski, Robson, and Bentley (2017) paragraph. Proposed text: "Nance (2022) also shows that circulating and in-library collections can be wellness infrastructure, not only recreational inventory. Academic libraries' circulating consoles, Lego build kits, coloring supplies, puzzles, Play-Doh, and kinetic materials give students flexible access to play-based breaks, social connection, and stress relief in the same spaces where they study."

# **Yap, J. M., & Peñaflor, J. (2020). The amazing library race: Developing students' media and information literacy skills through games. *Journal of Information Literacy, 14*(1), 66-82. https://doi.org/10.11645/14.1.2708**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `CB4XQXNR`; Better BibTeX citation key `yapAmazingLibraryRace2020`; attachment key `J4FKIFWH`.

Yap and Peñaflor present an illustrative case-study project report on how two academic libraries used game-based strategies to teach media and information literacy (MIL). The article begins from the problem of disinformation, misinformation, changing student learning behaviors, and the need for libraries to remain active champions of reliable information and lifelong learning. Their central claim is practical: games can be repurposed as interactive approaches that deliver information literacy instruction in a collaborative environment while also attracting students to library resources and services.

The authors distinguish gamification, game-based learning (GBL), serious games, and problem-based learning, while acknowledging that academic libraries often combine these approaches in orientation and instruction. They draw on the Amazing Library Race model as an upgraded scavenger-hunt format in which teams cannot proceed until they complete tasks tied to library spaces, resources, or competencies. Instead of treating play as decoration, the article maps race challenges onto UNESCO's three MIL components: recognizing information needs and accessing/retrieving information and media content; understanding, assessing, and evaluating information and media; and creating, using, and communicating information and media ethically.

The first case is Nazarbayev University Library in Kazakhstan, where the Library InfoLit Race was introduced in 2018. The race used pit-stop challenges to increase students' competencies in searching for reliable information, evaluating scholarly resources, citing and referencing ethically, and using library services. Sample tasks included arranging books by classification, identifying disciplines by class number, deciding when paraphrased text requires citation, assembling APA references, and using chat/reference services to locate research information through the library portal. The case also situates the race within a broader national and institutional MIL landscape that included UNESCO initiatives, IREX training, and Nazarbayev University's information literacy programming.

The second case is De La Salle University Libraries in the Philippines, where the IamInfoSMART campaign had developed since 2013. SMART stands for seeking the right information, managing retrieved information effectively, assessing information critically, referencing sources correctly, and treating/applying information ethically. The Amazing Race component led teams through library sections and pit-stop challenges around resource types, online library information, reference services, electronic databases, fake-news identification, citation games, and creative use of audiovisual collections. The article emphasizes that each task was designed to demonstrate a particular skill, not merely to entertain.

For the larger GBLS project, Yap and Peñaflor are useful because they show how relatively familiar library-game formats can be aligned with explicit MIL competencies across different national contexts. The race format combines physical movement, intellectual challenge, teamwork, clues, staff facilitation, and immediate task validation. It also demonstrates how library games can integrate orientation, database/resource discovery, classification, reference services, citation ethics, anti-plagiarism instruction, and misinformation evaluation in a single event structure. The cases therefore connect GBLS not only to engagement but to the public-service role of libraries as institutions that teach source evaluation and ethical information use.

The article's limits are also important. It is descriptive rather than an assessment study; evidence comes largely through practical examples and observations rather than pre/post learning data, comparison groups, or longitudinal transfer measures. Even so, it contributes implementation detail that is often missing from higher-level discussions of gamification. It names tasks, competencies, event structures, staff roles, and local adaptations, making it useful for GBLS design guidance. In the literature review, it should be treated as a concrete international academic-library example of problem-based, race-style MIL instruction rather than as proof that library races reliably improve learning outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after the Jerrett, Bothma, and de Beer (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Yap and Peñaflor (2020) add a two-country academic-library example of race-style, problem-based MIL instruction. Their cases at Nazarbayev University Library and De La Salle University Libraries mapped pit-stop challenges to UNESCO media and information literacy components, asking students to arrange books by classification, search library portals, use chat/reference services, evaluate fake news, identify source types, practice APA/MLA citation, avoid plagiarism, and communicate information ethically. The article is descriptive rather than evaluative, but it shows how a familiar Amazing Race/scavenger-hunt format can integrate orientation, information literacy, misinformation evaluation, citation ethics, teamwork, and staff validation into one reusable GBLS structure.

# **Keating, S., Lee, W.-C., Windleharth, T., & Lee, J. H. (2017). The style of Tetris is... possibly Tetris?: Creative professionals' description of video game visual styles. In *Proceedings of the 50th Hawaii International Conference on System Sciences* (pp. 2046-2055). https://doi.org/10.24251/HICSS.2017.248**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `AHBICLVT`; Better BibTeX citation key `keatingStyleTetrisPossibly2017`; attachment key `22HWDXT9`.

Keating, Lee, Windleharth, and Lee study video game visual style as an access point for game retrieval, cataloging, and cultural preservation. The article begins from a familiar problem in game metadata: users often can browse games by platform or genre, but those access points do not capture how games look, how their visual design is recognized, or how image-based qualities shape discovery. Existing library standards such as RDA, Dublin Core, and other bibliographic models were not built to describe game visual information in sufficient detail. Visual style is therefore treated as an overlooked but important part of game metadata.

The article is part of the University of Washington GAMER Group's larger work on the Video Game Metadata Schema (VGMS). Earlier VGMS work had developed a visual style taxonomy based largely on general game users. Keating et al. ask whether that taxonomy conforms to the language and cognitive descriptors used by people closer to game creation: video game art and design students at DigiPen Institute of Technology. This matters because metadata schemas must serve different user groups. A public-facing game catalog, a classroom collection, a game-studies research archive, and a game art/design program may need different degrees of specificity.

The method was a think-aloud card-sort activity with eight participants who were undergraduate or master's students in game art, design, or programming and active members of teams developing games. Participants sorted 48 screenshots representing a broad range of visual styles, created labels for their piles using their own language, and explained their sorting decisions aloud. The researchers recorded the activity, coded the language in NVIVO, and compared the resulting terms with the existing visual style taxonomy. The study produced 121 terms, including a long tail of unique descriptors used by only one participant.

Four prominent visual-style groups emerged: Abstract, Realistic, Stylized, and Text. Participants also used more specific subcategories of Stylized, including terms such as anime, cartoon, handicraft, Lego, clay/claymation, knitted/stitched, and yarn. A key finding is that expert or creator-adjacent users generated more Technique terms than general game players. They described visual styles through cel-shading, low-poly design, pixel art, 8-bit, 16-bit, 32-bit, and pre-rendering. Because the more cataloger-friendly version of the taxonomy had removed a Technique facet for simplicity, Keating et al. recommend restoring Technique as an optional facet for organizations serving expert users.

The study also shows that visual style does not stay neatly inside one metadata element. Participants frequently used terms that belonged to related VGMS elements such as dimension, gameplay genre, mood, point of view, and subject. They also used specific games or series as proxy terms for visual style, such as Tetris, Mirror's Edge, and Street Fighter. The title's Tetris example captures the problem: a participant could identify the image as distinct but struggled to name its style except by referring to the game itself. Visual style is therefore both useful and ambiguous; it can involve technique, visual mood, mechanics, motifs, dimensionality, cultural reference, and exemplar titles.

For the larger GBLS project, Keating et al. strengthens the cataloging and discovery argument. Game collections need metadata that reflects how patrons, students, artists, designers, scholars, and preservationists actually search and talk about games. Visual style could support advisory, curricular matching, exhibits, research collections, and preservation description, especially when users are looking for games that resemble a style, era, technique, or aesthetic rather than a platform or genre. The article also cautions that metadata depth should vary by context: public library catalogs may need broad visual-style terms, while academic game archives or design programs may benefit from deeper hierarchies and optional expert facets.

The limitations are clear. This is a small specialized-user study with eight DigiPen participants, based on screenshots rather than full play experiences, and it evaluates taxonomy language rather than testing a live library catalog. Still, its contribution to GBLS is strong because it translates abstract claims about game metadata into concrete design choices: controlled vocabularies need preferred and lead-in terms, near-synonym management, hierarchy, optional depth, and ways to handle temporal change. The proposed Intention facet, with values such as Retro, Futuristic, or Homage, is especially useful because game aesthetics change meaning over time; what was once technical limitation can later become intentional style.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Chapman (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Keating, Lee, Windleharth, and Lee (2017) make visual style a concrete game-metadata problem. In a think-aloud card sort with eight DigiPen game art, design, and programming students, participants generated 121 descriptors for 48 game screenshots, confirming broad categories such as Abstract, Realistic, Stylized, and Text while also surfacing expert-oriented Technique terms such as cel-shaded, low poly, pixel art, 8-bit, 16-bit, and pre-rendered. For GBLS, the study shows that visual-style metadata can support discovery, advisory, design education, and preservation, but it also cautions that schemas need optional depth because visual style overlaps with mood, dimension, point of view, gameplay genre, subject, exemplar titles, and temporally shifting intentions such as retro or homage.

# Monnier, R., & Winters, L. (2022). Playing in limbo: Using liminal space to promote library services and writing center. *Journal of Play in Adulthood, 4*(1), 32-49.

Unique identifier: `monnierPlayingLimboUsing2022`; Zotero item key `LLFJUUSX`; attachment key `ZFEYYAYD`.

Monnier and Winters (2022) describe how Pittsburg State University's Axe Library and writing center used playful activities in liminal spaces to promote engagement with library services, writing support, and student well-being after a major renovation and the disruption of COVID-19. The article is grounded in a specific spatial problem. The writing center, student success office, coffee shop, tutoring programs, collaboration spaces, and library resources were located in or around the main library, but students tended to use only the service they intentionally came for. The authors therefore focused on thresholds and undefined areas between services: places neither fully classroom nor social space, neither only writing center nor only library. They argue that those spaces can become sites of unscripted, creative activity through play.

The theoretical frame is liminality. Drawing on writing center studies and organizational space theory, the authors define liminal spaces as thresholds where different human worlds overlap and where new possibilities can emerge. Writing centers are already liminal because they combine instruction and service, safety and vulnerability, immediate help and long-term learning. Academic libraries are similarly ambiguous: they invite study, help-seeking, movement, collaboration, and social presence while also carrying expectations of quiet, seriousness, and individual academic work. This ambiguity can make students uncertain about how to engage. Monnier and Winters use play to lower that uncertainty and help students see the library as a campus home-away-from-home.

The article describes several interventions. In the writing center, staff first tried a "build a story" activity on an easel pad, but patrons rarely participated, possibly because the activity required visible writing and risked judgment. Coloring pages became more successful because they were low-concentration, easy to start and stop, portable, and usable while waiting for appointments. A LEGO table gave patrons tactile materials; some used pieces as fidgets, while a few chemistry students built molecular models. Origami was less successful for general use because instructions were difficult for beginners, but it fit better in the writing center's international-student conversation program, where creative activity supported language practice. Staff also used card games, Connect 4, and other two-player games to sustain conversation partners' English practice.

In the broader library, the authors describe passive and active play programming. Jigsaw puzzles near print queues and reading areas invited quick participation, reduced the awkwardness of waiting, and made the library feel like a place to work, relax, and spend time with friends. Coloring, glass-marker boards, oversized chess, checkers, International Games Week events, open gaming-group meetings, Dungeons & Dragons campaigns, a virtual escape room, virtual trivia, and Zoom game nights all helped test what kinds of play fit different spaces and pandemic conditions. The virtual escape room drew high engagement, with more than 700 clicks and more than 90 completed surveys.

Their exploratory user feedback during International Games Week 2021 was modest but useful. Patrons interviewed while using playful activities repeatedly described them as fun, asked for more games, and moved portable games to other areas such as Axe Grind so larger groups could play. Writing center employees reported using playful activities with conversation partners at least weekly, sometimes multiple times per week. The authors observed that patrons gravitated toward spaces where playful activities were staged and that movable games helped users cross into liminal areas they might otherwise avoid. Their future plans were deliberately simple: add more multiplayer games, add games near Axe Grind, and add more two-player games and activities to the writing center.

For the GBLS project, Monnier and Winters contribute an important space-and-facilitation perspective. Games do not have to be large, scripted programs to matter. Passive programming, portable materials, and visibly unfinished activities can reshape how patrons read an academic library's social permissions. Puzzles, coloring sheets, LEGO, cards, chess, checkers, and D&D can invite lingering, conversation, language practice, staff-patron contact, and service discovery. The article also reinforces that failed or partial experiments are informative: activities requiring too much cognitive effort, too much performance risk, or too many rules may not work well for patrons who are waiting, taking a break, or uncertain about whether play is acceptable in the space.

The limitations are clear. The interviews were informal, time-limited, affected by the pandemic, and biased toward people already engaging in play. The article does not measure learning, service uptake, writing outcomes, or long-term belonging. Even so, it provides a practical GBLS lesson: start simple, observe patron behavior, adapt materials to local spaces, and let playful opportunities make ambiguous service boundaries easier to cross.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Giles et al. (2019) paragraph. Proposed text: "Monnier and Winters (2022) extend belonging from orientation events to everyday academic-library thresholds. At Pittsburg State University, puzzles, coloring pages, LEGO, card games, D&D, International Games Week activities, and writing-center conversation games helped students treat liminal areas between the library, writing center, coffee shop, and student services as acceptable places for relaxation, language practice, social interaction, and low-stakes service discovery."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Build Partnerships and Community Pipelines`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Monnier and Winters (2022) show that GBLS partnerships can be spatial as well as organizational. Their library-writing center collaboration used small, portable, low-rule activities in threshold spaces to connect patrons with writing support, International Games Week, student organizations, conversation partners, D&D groups, and coffee-shop-adjacent gathering areas; the practical lesson is to start simple, observe where patrons move activities, and let play reveal where service boundaries feel inviting or uncertain."

# **Burek Pierce, J. (2016). The reign of children: The role of games and toys in American public libraries, 1876-1925. *Information & Culture, 51*(3), 373-398. https://doi.org/10.7560/IC51304**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `U72W4J3Y`; Better BibTeX citation key `burekpierceReignChildrenRole2016`; attachment key `RZ6X9FPA`.

Burek Pierce provides a historical argument that games, toys, pictures, puzzles, and other nonbook resources were not late twentieth- or twenty-first-century novelties in public libraries. In late nineteenth- and early twentieth-century children's rooms, librarians used games and tactile materials as part of serious youth services. The article is informed by the child study and kindergarten movements, both of which encouraged adults to understand children's developmental needs and to use sensory, visual, and creative materials to support learning. In that setting, children's librarians saw play, making, and handling objects as compatible with library service rather than as distractions from it.

The article revises book-centered histories of children's librarianship by examining professional discussions and material culture around nonbook resources. Burek Pierce shows that children were once barred or viewed cautiously in many public libraries, but the emergence of children's rooms created space for more child-centered professional practice. Librarians such as May Lowe imagined a "reign of the children" marked by colorful pictures, dissected maps, sliced animals, scrapbook-making, and other resources that would attract young users and transfer enthusiasm for pictures and playthings into enthusiasm for books, knowledge, and the library itself.

A major contribution is the detailed account of what early games and toys did pedagogically. Dissected maps required children to handle pieces, understand spatial relationships, master geography, and connect place names to visual form. Sliced Animals and related games combined image recognition, spelling, and assembly. Scrapbooks were both calming activities and creative productions, sometimes made by children for other children. Picture bulletins, stereoscopes, stereopticons, lantern slides, and realia brought visual and material learning into children's rooms. These examples show that early librarians were already designing multimodal learning environments, not simply adding amusements to book collections.

For GBLS, the article is especially useful because it frames playful library service as a long-running professional tension rather than a recent technology debate. Games and toys did not displace books in these early children's rooms; they often connected directly to books, stories, geography, history, art, and moral instruction. Burek Pierce also notes that when games moved from family settings into children's library rooms, they likely supported peer interaction among children as well as adult-guided learning. This history helps explain why contemporary libraries can treat games as part of access, learning, sociality, creativity, and public culture.

The article also explains why this early playful orientation receded. Some librarians questioned whether games and amusements belonged in libraries, and leaders such as Montrose J. Moses and Clara Whitehill Hunt pushed children's rooms toward the dignity, uniformity, efficiency, and book-centered seriousness associated with adult departments. Burek Pierce calls this opposition a banishing of "the experimental temptation": nonbook, colorful, playful services were increasingly treated as undisciplined or insufficiently professional. Later youth programs that used games tended to stress direct instruction, measurable improvement, and approved methods.

The application to the GBLS review is strong but historical. Burek Pierce does not assess modern game programs, circulation, or digital media. Instead, she supplies a professional genealogy: present-day arguments over apps, computer games, interactive books, toys, and atypical materials repeat older anxieties about whether playful, child-centered services support or dilute the library's mission. The article should be used in the introduction and historical frame to show that GBLS is not an abrupt break from librarianship but a recurring effort to align library service with children's agency, community needs, educational theory, and changing media forms.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Introduction: Why Game-Based Library Services Matter > The role of Libraries is everchanging`, replacing the existing Burek Pierce (2016) paragraph.

Suggested text: Today's game-based and multimedia library programs are not new; they continue a long-standing struggle to balance imagination and instruction, play and professionalism in library service. Burek Pierce (2016) shows that between 1876 and 1925, children's librarians used toys, games, pictures, maps, puzzles, scrapbook-making, lantern slides, and realia as child-centered service media informed by the child study and kindergarten movements. These resources did not displace books; they linked tactile, visual, social, and creative learning to reading, memory, curiosity, peer interaction, and a welcoming children's room. The later retreat from playful youth services came as library leaders sought efficiency, uniformity, dignity, and alignment with adult departments, treating the "experimental temptation" of nonbook media as a threat to professional seriousness. For GBLS, this history reframes games as a recurring public-library strategy rather than a digital-age novelty.

# Giles, K. (2015). No budget, no experience, no problem: Creating a library orientation game for freshman engineering majors. *The Journal of Academic Librarianship, 41*(2), 170-177. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.acalib.2014.12.005

Unique identifier: `gilesNoBudgetNo2015`; Zotero item key `EWFRJ3U8`; attachment key `2UCCMHZJ`.

Giles (2015) describes *Mystery at the Library*, a simple and inexpensive library-orientation game created for first-year engineering majors at James Madison University's Rose Library. The local problem was specific but common: engineering freshmen needed to know Rose Library's science, technology, and health collections, subject librarian offices, spaces, reserve materials, catalog, call-number system, and RefWorks, but the engineering curriculum offered little space for lower-level library instruction. The librarian wanted an out-of-class activity that would introduce the building, provide a shared experience before a later in-class session, and fit engineering students' preference for hands-on exploration.

The game adapted scavenger-hunt and alternate-reality-game ideas into a library mystery. Students were told that a fictional notebook belonging to Nikola Tesla had been stolen from a Rose Library display case. Suspect profiles, instructions, and backstory were distributed through the course website. Players began by finding the first clue on reserve, then followed photocopied citations, highlighted references, call numbers, and red-folder clues through the catalog, stacks, reference section, bound journals, and a thief's RefWorks account. The mystery structure replaced a worksheet with a narrative role: students were not simply answering a list of questions, but searching the library and online to identify the thief.

The design constraints are one of the article's strongest contributions. There was no budget for special materials, programming, video production, or actors, and the librarian had no previous game-design experience. The game therefore used existing library resources, class-web materials, paper clues, and red pocket folders costing less than a dollar each. It also had to be quiet, self-directed, and minimally disruptive to normal library operations. Desk staff tested the game before students played and later provided feedback that shaped revisions.

Giles is especially useful for GBLS because she reports iterative design changes. Early versions included a detective character, a blog and Gmail account, coded-message puzzles, and email interactions intended to add atmosphere. Player confusion and staff burden showed that these features were not worth keeping if they did not teach library skills. The game was simplified so that the challenge remained tied to learning objectives. A difficult task involving *The Dictionary of Energy* was retained because it revealed a real library-use problem: students did not understand that "Rose Lib-Reference" meant the first-floor reference section. The library responded by adding signage. Later revisions added a thief's "to do" list to clarify the structure and expand the game into the periodicals area.

The 2012 assessment compared two engineering course sections that played the game with a third control section that did not. Thirty-four of 44 students in the experimental group and 22 of 24 in the control group completed an online survey before any of the sections received in-class instruction. The control group reported a preference for learning by trying things and figuring them out. The experimental group reported greater familiarity with all listed tasks: getting reserve items, looking up books in the catalog, finding books on shelves, finding periodicals, finding the reference section, and logging in to RefWorks. The largest difference was RefWorks familiarity. Player response was favorable: 88% agreed that they learned about the library, 74% felt more comfortable using it, 71% felt more comfortable asking for help, 62% had fun, and 79% would recommend the activity for future classes.

For the GBLS project, Giles contributes a pragmatic orientation model. A low-cost library mystery can scaffold later instruction, make physical exploration meaningful, create reasons to interact with staff, reveal confusing service points, and turn existing collections into game materials. The case also cautions that atmosphere should not outweigh purpose. Narrative is useful when it structures action and creates continuity for later teaching, such as discussions of ethical information use, source evaluation, and search-term construction; extra puzzles or characters are distractions when they increase confusion without serving learning goals. The limitations are also clear: the study measures self-reported familiarity, not objective learning or long-term transfer, and it focuses on one engineering cohort at one institution. Still, it is a strong GBLS implementation source for libraries with limited money, staff, or game-design expertise.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Smith and Baker (2011) paragraph. Proposed text: "Giles (2015) provides a closely related freshman-engineering orientation case through *Mystery at the Library*, a no-budget paper-and-web mystery about a stolen Nikola Tesla notebook. The game used reserve materials, catalog searching, call numbers, reference and periodicals locations, red-folder clues, and a RefWorks account to move students through Rose Library; a small self-assessment comparison found higher reported familiarity with library tasks among players than nonplayers, while revisions showed that simple narrative and objective-aligned puzzles worked better than atmospheric complexity."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Design for Reflection and Transfer`, after the Guo and Goh (2016) paragraph. Proposed text: "Giles (2015) reinforces this design principle from a low-resource angle: keep the narrative simple, remove puzzles or characters that do not teach the service goal, retain challenges that reveal genuine library-use barriers, and use the game story afterward to bridge into source evaluation, ethical information use, search terms, and database limiters."

# **Cross, E., Mould, D., & Smith, R. (2015). The protean challenge of game collections at academic libraries. *New Review of Academic Librarianship, 21*(2), 129-145. https://doi.org/10.1080/13614533.2015.1043467**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `CBWVGQZ3`; Better BibTeX citation key `crossProteanChallengeGame2015`; attachment key `ANBVMHM3`.

Cross, Mould, and Smith examine the practical problem of academic-library game collections after the early wave of console-game collection building. Their core argument is that the gaming ecosystem had become more complex, so academic libraries supporting game development and game studies could not rely on a simple model of circulating console games. Instead, libraries needed to consider board games, console games, PC games, digital distribution, downloadable content, DRM, mobile games, MMOs, gaming labs, preservation, censorship, staff resistance, and traditional technical-services workflows.

The article is grounded in the Carleton University Library game collection, which began in 2008 after faculty requested games to support game-development courses. By the end of 2014, Carleton's collection included 119 board games, 550 console games, and 142 PC games available through Steam in a dedicated gaming lab. In the 2013-2014 reporting year, board games circulated 521 times and console games 1,645 times, with circulation distributed across much of the collection. Carleton also crowdsourced collection development by seeking input from faculty, students, and library staff.

One major contribution is the distinction among board, console, and PC games. Board games are valuable for aspiring game designers because rules and mechanics are visible, comparatively simple, and open to analysis. Console games are easy to shelve and circulate and include many high-production-value AAA titles that patrons want for course work and leisure. PC games, especially indie games, are important for game studies and design because they are experimental, political, artistic, and often more feasible as models for student projects; however, DRM and non-transferable licenses make ordinary lending difficult. Carleton responded by offering PC games through a bookable gaming lab rather than a circulation model.

The article also identifies several benefits of academic game collections. They support faculty and students in game development and game studies by making a game canon or course texts available on standard library terms. They enable teaching outside game programs, including digital history, gender studies, GIS Day demonstrations, Minecraft, Oculus Rift, and other interdisciplinary uses. The Carleton case also shows internal benefits: staff gaming clubs, conference presentations, improved morale, teamwork, and new professional expertise around an innovative library service.

The challenges are equally important for GBLS planning. Cross et al. describe a censorship challenge around *Rage*, which prompted review and public posting of a challenged-materials policy. They also describe internal resistance around cost, mature-rated content, and cumbersome or allegedly unhygienic accessories such as steering wheels, Wii balance boards, Dance Dance Revolution mats, and Rock Band instruments. Their conclusion is that game collections require institutional support, senior-management backing, collection policies, and communication with staff about how games support teaching and research.

For the larger GBLS project, this article is a strong operations and service-model source. It treats games as collections that require acquisition channels, credit cards for digital subscriptions, MARC records, emerging RDA/OLAC guidance, shelving, browsing, security, replacements, lost-item policies, preservation planning, technical-services capacity, room booking, lab acceptable-use policies, computing support, and ongoing attention to DRM/DLC. It also shows that game collections can be both circulating inventory and in-library infrastructure: the physical game, console, accessory, digital license, lab workstation, and staff expertise all become parts of access.

The article should be used as a practitioner case and planning framework rather than an outcomes assessment. Its evidence is a single academic-library case, circulation data, examples from Carleton courses, and implementation reflection. Still, its value to GBLS is high because it names the operational risks that make game services durable or fragile. Libraries that want to collect games need a collection-development policy that matches local curricula, local patrons, platform realities, preservation needs, and staff capacity; otherwise the collection can become too thin, too hard to access, or too vulnerable to challenge.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Circulating and In-Library Collections`, immediately after the Buller (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Cross, Mould, and Smith (2015) broaden the academic-library collection model through Carleton University Library's mix of board games, console games, Steam-based PC games, and a dedicated gaming lab. Their case shows why GBLS collections must account for format-specific access models: board games expose mechanics for design analysis, console games circulate easily but require platform and replacement decisions, PC/indie games may require lab access because DRM and non-transferable licenses break ordinary lending, and mobile games/MMOs may not fit library access at all. The article also makes operations visible, including challenged-materials policies, staff resistance, accessories, DLC, digital subscriptions, preservation, MARC/RDA cataloging, browsable shelving, replacement fees, room booking, acceptable-use rules, computing support, and senior-management backing.

# **Lee, J. H., Jett, J., & Perti, A. (2015). The problem of "additional content" in video games. In *Proceedings of the 15th ACM/IEEE-CS Joint Conference on Digital Libraries* (pp. 237-240). ACM. https://doi.org/10.1145/2756406.2756949**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `XJAX3WUX`; Better BibTeX citation key `leeProblemAdditionalContent2015`; attachment key `WY26SE5U`.

Lee, Jett, and Perti address a specific but increasingly central problem for video game collections: additional content such as mods and downloadable content (DLC). They argue that additional content creates philosophical and practical challenges for cultural heritage institutions on acquisition, description, access/use, and preservation. The issue is not limited to games, since serial publications, e-publications with datasets, and other digital or multimedia objects can also have supplementary or evolving content, but video games make the problem especially visible.

The article defines additional content carefully. Ordinary patches and bug fixes are excluded because they correct malfunctioning software rather than expand content. Additional content, in their usage, means content that improves or extends the player's experience beyond the game as initially purchased: new characters, dungeons, stories, maps, play modes, cosmetic assets, or other additions. The authors focus especially on mods, usually developed by fan communities, and DLC, usually produced and distributed by corporate entities. Both require the original game to be present, but they differ in responsibility, distribution, cost, and relation to community practice.

The philosophical challenge is the boundary of the work. In traditional bibliographic terms, a work is a distinct intellectual or artistic creation, but mods and DLC can substantially change gameplay and narrative. Optional additional content means two players may experience different versions of what is nominally the same game. In some cases, a mod can become better known than its source game, as with *Counter-Strike* and *Dota*. This raises a cataloging question that GBLS cannot ignore: when is additional content merely supplemental, and when does it become a new game or work?

The practical challenges are equally consequential. Acquisition is difficult because additional content may be time-limited, digitally distributed through platform-specific services, withdrawn, poorly documented by official distributors, incomplete in fan documentation, and dependent on possession of the original game. Selection is also difficult because fully acquiring all additional content for a game may be impossible or budget-breaking. Description is difficult because communities lack stable controlled vocabularies for different types of additional content, and mods or DLC may have different creators, genres, titles, system requirements, compatibility constraints, dependencies, and conflicts from their base games.

Access, use, and preservation become complicated because additional content can be delivered through physical media, separate folders, user profiles, encrypted console storage, platform accounts, or digital storefronts. Institutions may struggle to identify which files are the additional content, restore them to usable form, migrate them past copy protection, or preserve files that users never directly receive. The authors emphasize that a one-size-fits-all preservation framework is unlikely because platform architectures and access mechanisms vary so much.

Their proposed solution is a video game conceptual model that treats Additional Content as a separate entity published after the initial game. That entity can have attributes such as name/title, type (mod, DLC, expansion), and version/configuration requirements. The model could record, for example, that *Civilization V: Gods & Kings* is an expansion requiring the base game, while the *Camera Rotation* mod requires the base game, the expansion, and another mod. The authors acknowledge that this game-specific model departs from FRBR, but they argue that FRBR has difficulty with non-text media, serials, and multiple-version objects.

For the larger GBLS project, this paper strengthens the metadata, access, and preservation strand by showing that collecting games means collecting relationships, dependencies, versions, and contexts, not just discs or titles. DLC and mods affect what patrons can play, what scholars can cite, what preservationists can reconstruct, and what libraries can legally or technically provide. The article also warns that additional content creates perpetual cataloging: as games evolve, records and relationships must be maintained over time, making some games more analogous to serials than monographs.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Keating, Lee, Windleharth, and Lee (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Lee, Jett, and Perti (2015) add DLC and mods to the metadata problem by arguing that additional content challenges the boundary of the video game work itself. They distinguish content-expanding mods and DLC from ordinary patches, then show how additional content complicates acquisition, description, access/use, and preservation because it may have separate creators, titles, costs, platform dependencies, compatibility requirements, version conflicts, time-limited availability, encrypted storage, and unclear relationships to the base game. Their proposed game conceptual model treats Additional Content as a separate entity with attributes such as name/title, type, and version/configuration requirements; for GBLS, this shows that game description must capture evolving relationships and dependencies if libraries want patrons, researchers, and preservationists to understand what can actually be played or reconstructed.

# **Langan, K. A. (2021). The library language game: Information literacy through the lens of Wittgenstein's language games. *Communications in Information Literacy, 15*(1), 104-118. https://doi.org/10.15760/comminfolit.2021.15.1.6**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `LZXZ98KR`; Better BibTeX citation key `langinLibraryLanguageGame2021`; attachment key `ZCH5JX2B`.

Langan's article is a philosophical and critical information literacy essay about labeling, meaning, cataloging, power, and knowledge building. It uses Wittgenstein's idea of "language games" to argue that labels are not neutral handles attached to information. Instead, labels participate in meaning making: they shape how users understand, retrieve, group, and build knowledge from information. The article's central example is the relabeling of semi-automatic rifles from "assault weapons" to "weapons of war" in a newspaper headline, which Langan treats as an epistemological shift rather than a merely stylistic edit.

The article explains Wittgenstein's language games as contexts in which words have meaning only because participants understand the rules and uses of those words. Langan distinguishes naming from labeling: naming invents or bestows a term, while labeling adheres to an established concept. If participants do not understand the rules, contexts, implied meanings, or labels, the language game breaks down and "language goes on holiday." Langan brings this framework into library and information science by arguing that information seeking depends on the successful labeling of information.

The essay then links Wittgenstein to philosophy of information, especially Floridi's idea of the infosphere. In the library context, information has the potential to become knowledge only when it has been named and labeled in ways that carry meaning for participants in the library language game. Catalogers, interfaces, search algorithms, call numbers, subject headings, and metadata all help set the rules and contexts through which information seekers interact with information. Langan therefore treats cataloging broadly as an epistemological bridge between labels, information systems, and user meaning making.
