A major emphasis is critical librarianship. Langan argues that cataloging and information literacy are shaped by perspective, power, bias, social position, and privilege. The article discusses the Library of Congress subject heading "Illegal Aliens" as an example of how a label can encode a political and colonialist worldview. Drawing on Olson, Noble, critical race theory, critical librarianship, and related scholarship, the article asks instruction librarians to teach students to be mindful not only of information sources but also of the labels and systems that organize those sources.

For GBLS, the article is adjacent but not directly applicable. It is useful background for thinking about game cataloging, player vocabularies, genre labels, and critical information literacy because it foregrounds how labels shape access and interpretation. It could help explain why game metadata terms such as genre, platform, style, mood, or subject should be taught and questioned rather than treated as transparent. However, the article does not discuss library games, game-based learning, game collections, game programming, gamification, tabletop or video games, or playful library services. Its "language game" is Wittgenstein's philosophical concept, not a GBLS format.

Because the GBLS review already has stronger, directly relevant metadata and information-literacy sources, this article should not be added to `review.md`. It is best treated as explicitly excluded literature with a note that it may be useful only as broader critical cataloging or information literacy theory if the project later adds a theoretical appendix on naming, controlled vocabularies, and bias.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because the article is about Wittgensteinian language games, labeling, and critical information literacy rather than game-based library services.

# **Jaeger, P. T., Bertot, J. C., Thompson, K. M., Katz, S. M., & DeCoster, E. J. (2012). The intersection of public policy and public access: Digital divides, digital literacy, digital inclusion, and public libraries. *Public Library Quarterly, 31*(1), 1-20. https://doi.org/10.1080/01616846.2012.654728**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `LIJC52AS`; Better BibTeX citation key `jaegerIntersectionPublicPolicy2012`; attachment key `5XWB5QR3`.

Jaeger, Bertot, Thompson, Katz, and DeCoster examine how the concepts of digital divide, digital literacy, and digital inclusion have shaped Internet-related policy and public-library service. Their central concern is the gap between concepts and policies: public libraries are expected to provide Internet education, access, and assistance, but policy language often uses these terms inconsistently and does not reliably fund or include libraries in decision making. The article is therefore less a study of a particular library program than an information-policy argument about how public libraries became frontline institutions for public access to digital information.

The authors distinguish the three core terms. Digital divide names gaps in access to Internet technologies, broadband, hardware, and related infrastructure, often structured by socioeconomic status, education, geography, age, disability, language, and literacy. Digital literacy names the skills and abilities needed once access exists, including the ability to locate, evaluate, and use digital information. Digital inclusion is treated as the policy effort to combine high-speed Internet access with literacy, training, outreach, and service to unserved and underserved populations. The article stresses that these concepts shift as technology changes, so policy that focuses only on devices or broadband may miss participation, accessibility, affordability, training, and content-compatibility problems.

The policy history moves from the Communications Act of 1934 and the Telecommunications Act of 1996 to the American Recovery and Reinvestment Act, the Broadband Technology Opportunities Program, the Broadband Initiatives Program, and the FCC's National Broadband Plan. Jaeger et al. argue that U.S. policy has often prioritized technical means of access more than social conditions of access and literacy. They also criticize contradictions in policy, especially proposals that rely on public libraries as broadband and digital-literacy infrastructure while threatening programs such as E-Rate that help fund school and library connectivity.

Public libraries occupy the article's practical center. The authors cite national data showing that nearly all public libraries offer public Internet access, many are the only free public computer and Internet provider in their communities, and large numbers provide formal or informal technology instruction. Libraries offer public-access computers, Wi-Fi, one-on-one help, online-search training, database instruction, and employment-related assistance. The article emphasizes that patrons often come to libraries not only because they lack home access, but because they need skilled assistance with forms, government services, employment resources, and other online tasks.

The article's strongest claim is that libraries are being asked to solve digital-inclusion problems without matching governance, funding, or policy authority. Demand for public-access technologies, training classes, and online resources increased while many public-library budgets declined. Federal policies and initiatives acknowledged libraries as central to digital inclusion, yet libraries were often excluded from policy design and did not always receive direct support from broadband or workforce programs. The authors call for three changes: do not reduce library funding while demanding more digital-inclusion work, include public libraries in policy discussions and design, and build better interagency partnerships that recognize libraries as community-based intermediaries.

For the larger GBLS project, this article is useful background for thinking about equity, access, public technology, and the social responsibilities of libraries. It helps frame why game-based services involving VR headsets, gaming labs, consoles, virtual worlds, or digital literacy cannot be evaluated only as entertainment or novelty; they also sit inside broader questions of public access, affordability, accessibility, training, and staff capacity. However, the article does not discuss games, play, gamification, game-based learning, game collections, library gaming programs, or playful service design. It is a public-library digital-inclusion policy source rather than a GBLS source.

Because the review already includes more directly relevant sources on VR access, game collections, information-literacy games, and inclusive tabletop programming, Jaeger et al. should not be added to `review.md`. It is best retained as explicitly excluded background that may be useful later if the project develops a broader policy appendix on public access, digital inclusion, and library technology infrastructure.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because the article addresses public-library digital divide, digital literacy, digital inclusion, and policy infrastructure without discussing games or game-based library services.

# Bianchini, C., & Munini, P. (2025). ClaG: A classification system for plays and games in toy libraries and libraries. *JLIS.it, 16*(1), 73-89. https://doi.org/10.36253/jlis.it-624

Unique identifier: `bianchiniClaGClassificationSystem2025`; Zotero item key `ZTU5FAVS`; attachment key `A9F7WVQD`.

Bianchini and Munini (2025) present ClaG, a classification system for plays and games developed for the Archivio Italiano del Gioco (AIG), the Italian Games Archive in Udine. The article begins from a practical collection problem. AIG holds about 2,800 board games and more than 700 publications, and its game collection needs physical organization, cataloging, classification, and a usable arrangement for players, researchers, educators, and library staff. Existing local tags were not enough because they lacked notation, definitions, guidance, and a systematic structure suitable for organizing a physical collection.

The central claim is that game classification should be designed from the player's perspective. Bianchini and Munini adapt Ranganathan's account of bibliographic classification by asking how games can be arranged in a helpful sequence, replaced correctly after use, and placed meaningfully among already-held games. For a game library or a library with a game collection, classification should help staff and users select, research, identify, and deliver a game or set of games to a player or group of players. This makes the article directly relevant to GBLS infrastructure: classification is not just back-office order, but a service tool for matching games to local use.

Methodologically, ClaG was built through both top-down and bottom-up work. The top-down component drew on play and game literature, including Huizinga, Caillois, Salen and Zimmerman, Adams, game-design manuals, encyclopedias, dictionaries, and game scholarship. The bottom-up component involved recursively applying and revising candidate concepts while classifying more than 200 games, mainly table and board games. Game experts and volunteers tested preliminary versions, and the system was presented in two seminars where participants suggested revisions.

ClaG begins by distinguishing play, game, and toy. Play is treated broadly as a free, spontaneous activity with rules, boundaries, uncertain outcome, and fictitious character. Game is treated as a form of play involving rules, artificial conflict, arbitrary goals, and quantifiable outcomes. Toy is treated not as an object in itself but as material for play, especially mimicry play. From these definitions, the classification develops several major facets. The first is space, based on the player's position relative to the play-ground: play without a precisely defined space, real-scale play-ground, reduced-scale play-ground, and virtual play-ground. The second is materials, including games without materials, ball games, card games, dice games, tile games, paper-and-pencil games, and toys.

The third facet is setting: thematic games, non-thematic games, and simulation games. This matters because setting can drive educational selection, advisory, and curricular use. The fourth is game outcome, including solitaire, single winner, single winning team, cooperative, semi-cooperative, single loser, and no winner. The fifth is genre, built from Caillois's agon, alea, mimicry, and vertigo but elaborated for classification by focusing on player attitude and required skills. Agonistic games, for example, can be subdivided by language, storytelling, logic, mathematics, deduction, abduction, dexterity, strength, balance, coordination, speed, endurance, and other skill types. After expert feedback, a sixth facet, age, was added because librarians and toy-library practitioners considered suggested player age important for selection.

For the larger GBLS project, Bianchini and Munini are important because they connect game metadata, physical arrangement, advisory, and access. Many earlier metadata sources focus on video games, digital platforms, narrative description, or preservation artifacts. ClaG instead offers a classification approach for toy libraries and libraries with analog game collections, especially board and tabletop games. Its facets correspond to questions patrons and facilitators actually ask: Where can this be played? What materials are needed? Does it have a theme? How many players can win? Is it cooperative, competitive, or solitary? What skills or play attitudes does it require? What ages is it suitable for? These are service questions as much as cataloging questions.

The source should also be read with its limits in mind. The published article gives an overview rather than the full tables, and the current system is strongest for board games. Traditional children's games, sports, mimicry games, role-playing games, and video games are acknowledged as needing fuller development. Evaluation was preliminary, based on expert testing and seminar feedback rather than broad field deployment. Even so, the article gives the review a useful contemporary example of game classification as practical GBLS infrastructure and points toward future collaborative development through a Wikibase Cloud instance.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, after the Post et al. (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Bianchini and Munini (2025) extend game description from metadata records to shelf classification and player-facing selection through ClaG, a classification system for plays and games developed for the Italian Games Archive. Built through play/game theory, Ranganathanian classification principles, expert testing, and iterative classification of more than 200 mostly tabletop games, ClaG organizes games by space, materials, setting, game outcome, genre, and age so toy libraries and libraries with game collections can arrange, identify, research, and deliver games according to how patrons actually choose and use them."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Infrastructure and Metadata Gaps`, after the de Groat (2015) paragraph. Proposed text: "Bianchini and Munini (2025) show that infrastructure gaps also include the absence of practical classification systems for analog game collections. ClaG is promising because its facets map to use questions, but it still needs fuller treatment of traditional children's games, sports, mimicry and role-playing games, video games, large-scale field testing, and cross-institutional implementation."

# **Bayeck, R. Y., & Bayeck, J. M. (2024). The intersection of place and game design: The case of two classic African board games. *Design Issues, 40*(4), 5-16. https://doi.org/10.1162/desi_a_00776**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `F7G7R2IK`; Better BibTeX citation key `bayeckIntersectionPlaceGame2024`; attachment key `87XBIU83`.

Bayeck and Bayeck examine the interaction of place and game design through two classic African board games: Songo, associated here with Ekang/Fang communities in Cameroon, Gabon, and Equatorial Guinea, and Owela, associated with Aawambo communities in Namibia and Angola. The article begins from the observation that game design is often discussed through digital games, modern board games, and Western design vocabulary. The authors argue that historical and Indigenous games also deserve to be understood as designed artifacts, even when their original design process cannot be reconstructed.

A central move in the article is terminological and cultural. The authors resist flattening Songo, Owela, and related games into the generic label "Mancala," because that label hides distinctive names, rules, materials, philosophies, directions of play, and community meanings. They also acknowledge the difficulty of using English and Western concepts such as "board game" and "design" for African games whose playing surfaces may be carved wood, stone, or holes in the ground. They use these terms carefully, defining board games as games with playing pieces and surfaces that facilitate interaction among players, and defining design as a process of creation situated in time, place, situation, culture, materials, values, and local practice.

The theoretical framing connects game design, culture, place, and learning. Drawing on game-design scholarship, critical play, Indigenous game design, architectural and urban-design literature, and theories of place, the authors treat games as cultural artifacts and systems of information. Place is not simply a fictional setting inside a game; it is the physical, social, historical, and cultural where-ness in which the game is made and through which players make meaning. Place includes materials, landscapes, people, memories, symbols, histories, social relations, tangible heritage, and intangible heritage.

Methodologically, the article treats board games as texts and uses textual analysis to read Songo and Owela as cultural objects. The authors combine that textual analysis with semi-structured interviews with two game players, treating those interviews as paratexts that illuminate how players connect the games to history, ancestry, and community meanings. The evidence is therefore interpretive rather than experimental, but it is appropriate to the article's question: how place becomes visible in game aesthetics, materials, names, and player interpretation.

The Songo analysis shows how design draws on local environment and philosophy. Songo is described as a strategic game with a rectangular wooden board, two rows, fourteen holes, and seventy seeds known locally as Ibang. Its board material reflects forest environments, while the playing seeds come from Njansang, a locally important edible tree product used in food and livelihood practices. Player interpretation connects the fourteen holes and seven holes per player to Ekang/Fang ideas about time, work, planning, and life. In this reading, Songo is not only a strategy game; it encodes relationships among forest ecology, food practices, labor, philosophy, and social memory.

The Owela analysis makes a parallel argument for a different place. Owela is described as a strategic Aawambo game with four rows and thirty-two or more holes, originally dug into flat sandy ground and played with marula fruits called Eengobe, or cattle. The authors connect this design to semiarid plains, white sands, marula trees, agriculture, animal husbandry, and cattle as a sign of wealth. Even when Owela shifts from ground-dug holes to stone or wood forms in urban settings, its design remains tied to local materials, names, and lifeways. The article therefore treats analog game technologies as place-informed designs rather than neutral mechanics.

For the larger GBLS project, Bayeck and Bayeck are useful as adjacent game-studies evidence for culturally relevant game selection, advisory, programming, and facilitation. The article does not study libraries, but it challenges libraries to treat games as cultural media whose names, materials, histories, communities, and player meanings matter. A library that collects or programs classic, Indigenous, diasporic, or culturally specific games should avoid presenting them as interchangeable mechanics or generic "Mancala"-style activities. Description, display, facilitation, and teaching should respect local names, contexts, and knowledge, while also considering whether a game design is culturally meaningful for the learners and communities being served.

The limitations are important. The study analyzes two games, focuses largely on visual and material design, and uses only two player interviews. It does not evaluate a library program, circulation collection, game-based learning intervention, or patron outcome. Still, it gives the GBLS review a concise way to connect analog tabletop services with cultural memory, place-based design, and inclusion. It supports the broader claim that library game services need culturally attentive advisory and facilitation, not only access to a shelf of game objects.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, immediately after the Cross et al. (2023) paragraph.

Suggested text: Bayeck and Bayeck (2024) add a culturally situated game-design caution for GBLS selection and facilitation. Their textual analysis of Songo and Owela argues that classic African board games should not be flattened into generic "Mancala" labels; local names, materials, spaces, directions of play, seeds, holes, cattle metaphors, forest and semiarid landscapes, and community philosophies all shape what the games mean and how players connect them to ancestry, memory, and place. The article is adjacent rather than library evidence, but it supports culturally relevant game advisory: libraries should describe, select, teach, and facilitate culturally specific tabletop games with attention to community knowledge and learner context rather than treating them as interchangeable mechanics.

# **Chow, A. S., Baity, C. C., Zamarripa, M., Chappell, P., Rachlin, D., & Vinson, C. (2012). The information needs of virtual users: A study of Second Life libraries. *The Library Quarterly, 82*(4), 477-510. https://doi.org/10.1086/667436**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WVYERW8X`; Better BibTeX citation key `chowInformationNeedsVirtual2012`; attachment key `5RMF8YWS`.

Chow, Baity, Zamarripa, Chappell, Rachlin, and Vinson examine whether libraries should provide information services to virtual patrons in Second Life and how well existing virtual library branches match user needs. The article treats virtual worlds as social, three-dimensional environments that were attracting libraries, colleges, universities, and volunteer librarians, but it is skeptical of simply reproducing physical library services in a simulated building. Its central question is a usability question: are virtual libraries useful and usable for the patrons and librarians who encounter them?

The study uses a mixed-method design centered on the Olathe Public Library in Kansas and its Second Life branch, while also gathering data from other Second Life librarians, Second Life library patrons, and general Second Life users. The authors conducted interviews with Olathe library leaders, surveyed Olathe's Second Life committee, surveyed real-life Olathe patrons, ran a focus group with Olathe patrons, surveyed Olathe's Second Life library users, surveyed patrons at several Second Life libraries, surveyed general Second Life users, and interviewed four well-known Second Life librarians. Across these components, 366 librarians, patrons, and Second Life users participated.

The literature review usefully situates Second Life libraries as an early virtual-world service experiment. The authors distinguish virtual branches tied to real-world public libraries from "born virtual" libraries such as the Alliance Virtual Library and Caledon Library System. They also name a key design shift: virtual-world libraries are less effective when they are object-oriented repositories of static links or text, and more promising when they become experience-oriented environments with events, exhibits, reference help, classes, community spaces, and three-dimensional demonstrations that cannot easily happen in a physical branch.

The Olathe case shows the organizational fragility of this work. Olathe's Second Life branch began through the passion and initiative of a librarian already familiar with the platform, then gained director approval and a nine-person committee. The project was exploratory, intended to prepare the library for emerging technologies and reach virtual users, but it had no formal management structure. Much of the labor occurred on employees' own time, committee support was mixed, and the learning curve was high for staff and patrons. The authors repeatedly connect these practical problems to usability: if a service depends on volunteer time, peer skepticism, unclear work status, and limited promotion, it is difficult to make efficient, effective, satisfying, and sustainable.

The user findings are especially important for GBLS. Real-life Olathe patrons mostly used the library for books, videos, and general information; 84.7 percent did not know the library had a Second Life branch, and interest in a virtual branch was modest until patrons saw a demonstration. Once they saw the environment, focus-group participants became more interested in virtual tours, dramatic reenactments, plays, audiobooks, documentaries, and in-world reference. In contrast, Second Life library patrons were often new or first-time visitors, were usually exploring rather than seeking a specific resource, rarely talked with a librarian, and often did not use resources while visiting. This means virtual users were not simply remote versions of local public-library patrons.

The study's broad Second Life patron survey found that most respondents had used Second Life for less than a month, most were first-time visitors to the library they encountered, and many discovered the library by exploring. Second Life-only libraries drew more responses than virtual branches of traditional libraries, but the study did not identify a single resource or service that would reliably attract users. Interviews with active Second Life librarians pointed instead to community as the central value. These librarians described virtual libraries as places for networking, collaboration, classes, lectures, building/object-creation help, in-world reference, job-seeking assistance, live music, storytelling, book clubs, and geographically dispersed professional connection.

For the larger GBLS project, Chow et al. provide a strong early empirical anchor for programs inside game-like virtual worlds. Second Life is not a game in the same way as a bounded videogame, but the article repeatedly shows that its value for libraries depended on play-like exploration, avatar presence, in-world community, immersive demonstrations, and a culture that outsiders sometimes dismissed as "just a game." The lesson is not that libraries should build virtual branches by default. Rather, virtual-world services need clear audience analysis, visible promotion, staff time, technical support, community participation, and platform-specific services that use the virtual world's affordances instead of duplicating physical spaces.

The limitations are substantial but clear. Only one traditional public library and its virtual branch were studied closely; the research was limited to Second Life; and the general nonlibrary Second Life user sample was small. The platform itself also had steep hardware and skill barriers, so it did not necessarily bridge the digital divide. Even so, the article remains useful for GBLS because it distinguishes novelty from service fit: successful virtual library work depends on matching virtual form to virtual user behavior, designing for social engagement and experiential learning, and treating staff labor and organizational legitimacy as part of the service model.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Bell and Trueman (2008) paragraph.

Suggested text: Chow et al. (2012) provide empirical grounding for this early Second Life moment through a mixed-method usability and needs assessment involving Olathe Public Library, its Second Life branch, other virtual librarians, and 366 total participants. They found a weak match between traditional public-library users and virtual users: local patrons were often unaware of or only modestly interested in Second Life until shown examples, while virtual library visitors were frequently first-time explorers with diffuse information needs who rarely used static resources or spoke with librarians. The study suggests that virtual-world library services work best when they emphasize community, events, exhibits, in-world help, classes, social connection, and experiences not possible in physical branches, while also warning that volunteer labor, limited staff time, platform learning curves, promotion gaps, and the perception that Second Life is "just a game" make sustainability difficult.

# Capdarest-Arest, N., Opuda, E., & Stark, R. K. (2019). "Game on!" Teaching gamification principles for library instruction to health sciences information professionals using interactive, low-tech activities and design-thinking modalities. *Journal of the Medical Library Association, 107*(4), 566-571. https://doi.org/10.5195/jmla.2019.636

Unique identifier: `capdarest-arestGameTeachingGamification2019`; Zotero item key `7XLJGKK4`; attachment key `DEEDAEAC`.

Capdarest-Arest, Opuda, and Stark (2019) report on "Design, Play, Learn," a ninety-minute special content session at the 2018 Medical Library Association annual meeting designed to teach health sciences information professionals how to create low-tech games for library instruction. The article is not an evaluation of student learning from a library game; instead, it is professional-development evidence about how librarians can learn gamification principles, design-thinking methods, and rapid prototyping practices. This distinction matters for the GBLS review because staff confidence, training, and design capacity are recurring implementation barriers.

The article frames low-tech gamification as an accessible alternative to high-tech games. High-tech games may require programming skills, software, funding, devices, and time that many instruction librarians and learners do not have. Low-tech games, including analog card, board, and classroom games, can be prototyped quickly, played face to face, and tested with ordinary materials. The authors pair this low-resource orientation with design thinking: empathize, define, ideate, prototype, and test. For library instruction, that process means identifying recurring instructional problems, brainstorming game-based solutions, building prototypes, and testing whether the game helps address the original teaching need.

The session itself combined flipped preparation, short didactic instruction, active learning, and team-based making. Participants received suggested readings, a short presentation on gamification, a behavior agreement, a shared Google Drive folder, and a sample low-tech card game for database-search instruction. The sample game asked health sciences students to practice creating database search strings using Boolean operator cards and health-specific terminology cards. During the session, attendees worked in self-selected groups of two to eight with pens, markers, colored paper, index cards, dice, rubber bands, scissors, paper clips, and other ordinary supplies. They could adapt the sample game or design their own, write rules, create game pieces, take photos, and upload materials to a shared folder for reuse under a Creative Commons model.

The reported assessment is modest but useful. Forty-three participants completed the pre-session survey and 35 completed the post-session survey, although the authors could not calculate a return rate because attendees could enter and leave freely. Before the session, 12 of 43 respondents (28%) reported feeling confident using gamification in a library instruction setting; after the session, 29 of 35 respondents (83%) did. The percentage planning to use what they learned in future instruction rose from 77% to 94%, and the percentage planning to use the sample game or a game built during the session rose from 46% to 86%. Qualitative feedback suggested that participants found the session helpful, wanted more time to play and debrief, and made connections for future collaboration.

For the larger GBLS project, the article helps define facilitation and professional preparation as infrastructure. Libraries cannot scale game-based instruction simply by collecting examples of games; staff need ways to practice game design, test rules, prototype with constraints, assess learning, and adapt ideas to local classrooms. The article's emphasis on low-tech materials is especially important for equity and sustainability. It shows that professional development can lower the threshold for experimentation by making game design tactile, collaborative, iterative, and achievable inside a short workshop.

The article's limits are clear. The surveys measure self-reported confidence and intention, not whether participants later implemented games or whether those games improved student learning. The session was informal, attendance was fluid, and the sample was limited to MLA conference attendees. Still, the case is valuable because it documents a replicable training format for librarians: short reading preparation, brief conceptual framing, shared materials, a sample game, hands-on group prototyping, informal testing, shared repositories, and follow-up plans. For GBLS, this is evidence that training design itself can be game-like, low-cost, and practice-oriented.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Professional Practice and Training Gaps`, after the Elkins and Hollister (2020) paragraph. Proposed text: "Capdarest-Arest, Opuda, and Stark (2019) make professional preparation more concrete through a ninety-minute MLA workshop that taught health sciences librarians low-tech game design and design-thinking methods. Participants used ordinary materials to prototype database-instruction games, and self-reported confidence using gamification rose from 28% before the session to 83% after it; the study is short-term and self-reported, but it shows that GBLS training can focus on rapid prototyping, rule testing, shared repositories, and low-cost instructional design rather than only on finished game examples."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Treat Facilitation as Core Infrastructure`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Capdarest-Arest et al. (2019) also show that facilitation capacity can be taught through the same playful methods GBLS uses with patrons. Their low-tech workshop positioned librarians as designers and testers, giving them practice with empathizing, defining instructional problems, ideating, prototyping, testing, and adapting games with accessible materials."

# **Barz, N., Benick, M., Dörrenbächer-Ulrich, L., & Perels, F. (2024). The effect of digital game-based learning interventions on cognitive, metacognitive, and affective-motivational learning outcomes in school: A meta-analysis. *Review of Educational Research, 94*(2), 193-227. https://doi.org/10.3102/00346543231167795**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FLHZXQRL`; Better BibTeX citation key `barzEffectDigitalGameBased2024`; attachment key `TEZZ9AE2`.

Barz, Benick, Dörrenbächer-Ulrich, and Perels offer a theory-based meta-analysis of digital game-based learning (DGBL) interventions in school settings. Their starting point is that previous meta-analyses covered broad time spans and many target groups, making it difficult to transfer findings to pupils in contemporary schools. The authors therefore focus on studies published between 2015 and 2020, a period when digital devices, game development tools, memory, graphics, and school technology infrastructure had changed enough to warrant a more current synthesis.

The article defines DGBL as the use of complete digital games or simulations on computers or mobile devices for teaching and learning. This definition is intentionally narrower than much GBLS work. The authors excluded analog games, role play, card games, pure gamification with only a single game element, Kinect-style embodiment, augmented reality, and virtual reality because those formats involve different qualities of interaction or immersion. They also limited the target population to pupils in grades 1-13, ages 6-18, and excluded university students, adult learners, clinical settings, workplaces, and special subpopulations.

The inclusion criteria were strict. Studies had to use at least a quasi-experimental pre-post-control design, include a traditional-instruction or no-intervention control group, occur in a school setting, measure at least one cognitive, metacognitive, or affective-motivational outcome, use quantitative methods, report enough statistics to calculate effect sizes, and appear as peer-reviewed journal articles or conference proceedings in English or German. The search drew from PubMed, ProQuest, IEEE, ACM, Web of Science, EBSCOhost, and Google Scholar. After title, abstract, and full-text screening, the final synthesis included 35 studies after excluding one outlier.

The final sample represented 7,139 participants with an average age of 10.89 years and an average grade level around sixth grade. The authors coded study year, location, sample characteristics, grade, number of sessions, additional non-game instruction, intervention duration, game type, playing mode, competition, dimensionality, visual realism, narration, content domain, avatars, digital agents, and statistical data. Seven educational-science experts categorized outcomes as cognitive, metacognitive, or affective-motivational. Effect sizes were converted to Hedges' g, and random-effects meta-analyses and meta-regression models were used because DGBL interventions varied substantially.

The findings partially support DGBL in schools. The overall learning effect was significant and medium-sized, g = .54, with 91 percent of observed estimates positive. Cognitive learning outcomes showed a significant medium effect, g = .67, and all observed cognitive estimates were positive. Affective-motivational learning outcomes showed a significant but small effect, g = .32. Metacognitive outcomes, also g = .32, were not statistically significant, largely because only five studies measured metacognitive learning. The authors found no evidence of publication bias after using fail-safe N, funnel plots, Egger's regression test, and selection models.

The moderator results are also useful. The authors did not find significant moderator effects for age, number of sessions, competition, dimensionality, visual realism, narrative, avatars, digital agents, additional non-game instruction, game type, playing mode, learning domain, publication year, or country of data collection. They caution that this may reflect very high heterogeneity, small subgroup sizes, and incomplete reporting rather than proof that design features do not matter. The discussion emphasizes that DGBL only motivates effectively when learning content and engaging game mechanics are thoughtfully balanced.

For the larger GBLS project, Barz et al. should be used as background evidence rather than direct library evidence. It supports the general claim that well-designed digital game-based learning can improve cognitive learning and modestly improve affective-motivational outcomes for school-aged learners compared with traditional instruction. It also reinforces several GBLS cautions: game format should be matched to outcomes, cognitive gains should be separated from motivation and metacognition, implementation requires device access and staff or teacher gaming literacy, and heterogeneity makes it risky to say that games work uniformly across contexts.

The article's limits matter for placement. It does not examine libraries, public programming, collections, informal learning, teen services, tabletop games, gamification, VR, AR, or virtual-world programs. Its results cannot be treated as evidence that any library game program will improve learning. However, it gives the review a credible educational-research anchor for why libraries might take game-based learning seriously, while also clarifying that GBLS needs its own field-specific evidence on audiences, formats, implementation, equity, and outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Introduction: Why Game-Based Library Services Matter > Importance > Games have demonstrated value in education, health, culture, entertainment`, immediately after that heading.

Suggested text: Barz et al. (2024) provide broad educational evidence for taking game-based learning seriously, while also showing why GBLS cannot borrow that evidence uncritically. In a meta-analysis of 35 school-based digital game-based learning studies published from 2015 to 2020, representing 7,139 pupils, DGBL produced a significant medium overall learning effect (g = .54), a significant medium cognitive effect (g = .67), a significant small affective-motivational effect (g = .32), and no significant metacognitive effect. Because the review excluded analog games, gamification, VR/AR, university/adult learners, and non-school settings, it is best used as background support for the educational promise of well-designed games, not as direct evidence for library programs.

# Adetayo, A., Oladipo, Y., Adeleke, O., & Odunewu, A. (2023). Gamification of cataloguing and classification education in Nigeria library and information science schools: A technology-enhanced approach. *Journal of Education for Library and Information Science, 64*(4), 370-385. https://doi.org/10.3138/jelis-2022-0028

Unique identifier: `adetayoGamificationCataloguingClassification2023`; Zotero item key `UXSNV642`; attachment key `RJAYNGHA`.

Adetayo, Oladipo, Adeleke, and Odunewu (2023) examine gamification as a possible response to difficulties in cataloguing and classification education in Nigerian library and information science schools. The article begins from a professional-education problem: cataloguing and classification are foundational to librarianship, but they are often experienced by Nigerian LIS students as technical, difficult, theory-heavy, and disconnected from practice. The authors argue that this negative perception matters because students who already see librarianship as "books and boring" may become further discouraged when core technical courses are taught through traditional methods.

The article defines gamification as the use of game elements in non-game settings to create motivational experiences, not as the creation of a full game. Drawing on self-determination theory, the authors distinguish intrinsic motivation from extrinsic motivation and frame gamification as a way to increase attention, engagement, safe practice, feedback, collaboration, and progress. They also identify three broad design orientations: immersion-based game design, social-based design, and achievement/challenge-based design. For cataloguing and classification education, the most relevant parts are visible objectives, short tasks, progress feedback, manageable challenges, rewards, and peer support.

Much of the article is a conceptual review rather than a field test. After surveying literature on gamification, cataloguing, classification, Nigerian LIS education, and cloud computing, the authors propose a model for technology-enhanced gamification of cataloguing and classification instruction. The model is built around five requirements: motivation, short-term tasks, a reward system, task design, and teamwork. The proposed task flow breaks cataloguing and classification into smaller stages: understanding tools, describing items, assigning subject access, producing a class mark, and placing the item correctly. A classification exercise, for example, might ask students to drag and drop books into Library of Congress classes and earn points for correct choices.

Cloud computing is presented as the enabling technology because it can provide infrastructure, platforms, and software without requiring every school to upgrade local computing resources. The authors mention services such as Google Cloud Platform, Microsoft Azure, Alibaba, IBM Bluemix, and Amazon Web Services as possible deployment environments, with a smaller nod to affective computing. Their point is not that one specific platform is necessary, but that cloud delivery could allow repeated practice, continuity across sessions, scalable access to learning modules, and a shared backend for game features and learning content.

For the larger GBLS project, the article is useful because it links game-based design directly to a library-specific technical curriculum. Many library-gaming sources address orientation, information literacy, reading, programs, or collections; this one asks how play can help future librarians learn the metadata and organization work that makes library collections usable. It therefore connects GBLS to cataloging, classification, professional preparation, and the infrastructure behind discovery. It also reinforces a design lesson that appears across the review: gameful systems work best when complex tasks are decomposed into smaller, meaningful challenges with feedback and opportunities to retry.

The article's limits are important. The authors explicitly state that there are few or no empirical studies on gamification of cataloguing and classification in Nigerian library schools, so the model relies on gamification evidence from other educational contexts. They also warn that high-quality gamified modules have hidden costs, including design, testing, assessment, consultation with course specialists, and technology adoption. Faculty may need workshops and support before they can use gamification tools effectively. The article therefore should not be treated as evidence that a particular game improved cataloguing learning outcomes; it is better used as a proposed framework and as evidence of a professional-training and assessment gap.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, after the Bianchini and Munini (2025) paragraph. Proposed text: "Adetayo, Oladipo, Adeleke, and Odunewu (2023) move cataloging and classification from collection description into LIS education by proposing a gamified model for teaching Nigerian students cataloguing tools, descriptive work, subject access, class marks, and shelving logic. Their model uses cloud delivery, short-term tasks, rewards, scaffolded task design, and teamwork to make technical metadata labor more practice-based and motivating, though it remains a conceptual framework rather than a tested intervention."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Professional Practice and Training Gaps`, after the Capdarest-Arest, Opuda, and Stark (2019) paragraph. Proposed text: "Adetayo et al. (2023) broaden the training gap from practicing librarians to LIS students and faculty. Their proposed gamified cataloguing and classification model assumes that students need repeated practice and motivational scaffolding, but it also notes that faculty may need technology training, institutional support, and stakeholder coordination before such systems can be implemented in Nigerian library schools."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Evidence Gaps`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Adetayo et al. (2023) illustrate a common evidence gap in GBLS-adjacent literature: a detailed gameful model may be theoretically plausible and locally relevant, but still lack classroom testing, learning-outcome data, cost analysis, and implementation evidence. Their cataloguing and classification proposal is therefore useful as a design agenda, not yet as proof of effectiveness."

# **Madray, A., & Catalano, A. (2010). The Curriculum Material Center's vital link to play and learning: What's the connection? *Education Libraries, 33*(2), 11-17.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `47TXRCQV`; Better BibTeX citation key `madrayCurriculumMaterialCenters2010`; attachment key `CEXPNSXI`.

Madray and Catalano connect children's play, learning, cognitive development, and Curriculum Materials Center (CMC) collection work. Writing as academic librarians responsible for information literacy, education liaison work, and the management and collection development of a CMC, they argue that play-related materials in schools, homes, and libraries are vital to learning. The article is a professional-practice essay rather than an assessment study, but it is directly useful for GBLS because it treats games, toys, manipulatives, puzzles, realia, and related resources as legitimate library instructional materials.

The opening literature frame draws on Froebel, Fred Rogers, Piaget, Vygotsky, Bodrova and Leong, and other child-development and educational-psychology sources. The authors emphasize that play supports neurological, cognitive, physical, motor, language, social, emotional, self-help, problem-solving, leadership, and literacy development. Their argument is not that play is extra decoration around learning, but that play and learning operate as co-equals in childhood development. This frame helps explain why CMCs and education libraries might collect playful materials alongside books and curriculum guides.

The article then situates this work historically in Curriculum Materials Centers, formerly Curriculum Laboratories. A CMC is described as a physical location within an academic library or teacher-education campus where pre-service and in-service teachers can experiment with, evaluate, and use instructional materials. The authors link CMCs to National Council for Accreditation of Teacher Education requirements and to ACRL/EBSS collection guidance. Recommended CMC resources include flash cards, music, games, puzzles, pictures, books on tape, big books, little books, models, videos, charts, maps, manipulatives, realia, toys, computer software, and board and psychoeducational games.

A central practical claim is that games and manipulatives are among the most popular CMC materials across P-12 content areas. These materials support multiple forms of skill building and appear in English language arts, geography, history, science, math, fine arts, foreign languages, counseling, physical education, and special education. For GBLS, this matters because the article locates game-based service not only in youth programming or leisure collections, but also in academic-library support for teacher preparation, standards-aligned lesson design, and classroom practice.

The subject-area examples show how wide the service model can be. Math learning can involve water play, block play, card games, musical chairs, group games, board games, Pizza Fractions, and Unifix blocks; games can make repetition more intrinsically motivated than worksheets and provide immediate feedback. Science learning can use thermometers, scales, microscopes, telescopes, rocks, shells, blocks, water play, experiments, racing games, and other active-learning tools. Social studies can use role play, geography globes, Monopoly, Civilization, Oregon Trail, Pandemic, The Making of the President, and other board or electronic games. Music, physical education, special education, and children's literature are also treated as domains where play, movement, pretend play, story play, and accessible toys can support development.

The collection-development guidance is especially relevant to libraries. Madray and Catalano stress that CMC librarians should not merely buy and house games and toys. They should choose materials that fit classroom time constraints, age levels, content areas, state and national standards, different learning styles, and the practical realities of circulation. They caution against highly technical items, special batteries, or many small pieces that may be lost. They recommend collaboration with education faculty, subject-area conferences, literature review, journals such as *School Library Journal*, vendor catalogs, and attention to discovery learning, creativity, problem solving, and developmentally appropriate design.

The article also treats promotion and instruction as part of collection work. CMC librarians can create displays of toys and books by content area, demonstrate games and toys during library instruction, offer workshops on play and selecting developmentally appropriate toys, host open houses, and support teacher-created materials. The conclusion is that educators can create games where they do not yet exist, but that requires understanding learning theory, child development, subject matter, and classroom use. The CMC librarian's role is therefore advisory and instructional as much as custodial.

For the larger GBLS project, this source is useful because it provides a collection-based model for play-oriented educational service. It predates much recent GBLS language, but it shows that academic libraries serving education programs have long collected and taught with playful learning materials. Its limitations should be kept visible: it is a conceptual and professional-practice article, not a study of outcomes, circulation, patron demand, or implementation barriers. Still, it strengthens the review's argument that game-based library service includes curricular collections, teacher preparation, manipulatives, standards-aligned selection, and librarian-led guidance on how games and toys become learning tools.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Collections, Circulation, and Access`, immediately after the Buchanan and Vanden Elzen (2012) paragraph.

Suggested text: Madray and Catalano (2010) add a Curriculum Materials Center model for game-based collections in academic libraries serving teacher education. They treat games, puzzles, toys, manipulatives, realia, computer software, board games, and psychoeducational games as instructional materials that help pre-service and in-service teachers connect play to P-12 learning across math, science, social studies, music, physical education, special education, and children's literature. Their practical guidance makes the CMC librarian an advisor as well as collector: select materials with faculty input, fit them to age levels, standards, classroom time, learning styles, durability, and circulation realities, and promote them through displays, instruction, workshops, open houses, and support for teacher-created games.

# Šiptar, D. (2023). Pavey, S. *Playing games in the school library: Developing game-based lessons and using gamification concepts*. *Vjesnik bibliotekara Hrvatske, 66*(2), 220-223.

Unique identifier: `siptarPLAYINGGAMESSCHOOL2023`; Zotero item key `VAVSQVVX`; attachment key `P7YFM65D`.

Šiptar (2023) reviews Sarah Pavey's *Playing Games in the School Library: Developing Game-Based Lessons and Using Gamification Concepts*, a practitioner handbook for school librarians interested in game-based learning and gamification. The review is written in Croatian and is not itself an empirical study; its value for the GBLS project is that it summarizes a current school-library practice text and identifies the range of lesson types, design models, learner considerations, and collaboration issues that Pavey treats as central to school-library game work.

The review emphasizes that Pavey distinguishes learning through play from gamification. Learning through play involves a process of playing that also seeks learning outcomes, while gamification may use game elements such as rewards, points, or leaderboards without requiring a full game. This distinction matters across the GBLS review because many library projects labeled as games are actually gameful instruction systems, and many game-based lessons mix formal play with points, rewards, narrative, or challenge structures.

Šiptar reports that the book begins with pedagogical theories connected to learning, including behaviorism, cognitive constructivism, social constructivism, model-based learning, multiple intelligences, and brain-based learning. Pavey also uses evaluative frameworks such as SAMR, ARCS, and Bloom's taxonomy to help librarians judge whether game-based lessons are doing more than adding novelty. The review repeatedly notes that the book combines theory with concrete examples from school librarians around the world, which makes it more usable as a practice guide than a purely abstract discussion of games and learning.

The game formats summarized in the review are broad. Pavey begins with simple games made through presentation tools so the approach remains accessible to librarians who may lack advanced technical skills. She then discusses digital games, coding games, catalog-system games, social-media games, smart-device apps, and commercial templates. Later chapters cover games with physical components, including everyday objects, quizzes, cards, and board games; mixed-media games that combine physical play with digital support such as QR codes, 3D models, GPS, or audio/video learning; and active or live games that rely on movement, communication, empathy, problem solving, larger spaces, and substantial preparation.

The review is especially useful for GBLS because it foregrounds learner diversity. Šiptar notes Pavey's attention to students who are disengaged, afraid of failure, learning in a non-native language, dyspraxic, autistic, competitive, shy, or low in self-confidence. This turns school-library games into a question of inclusion and adaptation, not just lesson novelty. The review also notes practical constraints: access to devices, student age and maturity, language availability of digital tools, costs of recommended resources, and the fact that some examples are rooted in the United Kingdom and may need local adaptation.

The later chapters summarized by Šiptar connect GBLS to school-wide service models. Pavey discusses how game-based learning and gamification can involve students beyond formal classes and curriculum obligations, how materials and activities can be shared with other schools, and how school librarians can collaborate with teachers, administrators, and parents. The review highlights curriculum knowledge, participation in curriculum design, and presentation of outcomes as ways librarians can gain support for new or improved game-based activities.

For the larger GBLS project, Šiptar's review is a compact pointer to practitioner knowledge in school libraries. It should not be used as direct evidence that a particular program works, because it summarizes a handbook rather than reporting original outcomes. Still, it helps the literature review represent school-library practice, especially the need to align game form with learning goals, select technologies that fit local constraints, adapt activities for diverse learners, collaborate with teachers and school leadership, and document results in ways that make future support more likely.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Giles (2015) paragraph. Proposed text: "Šiptar's (2023) review of Pavey's school-library handbook extends instructional GBLS into K-12 practice by distinguishing learning through play from gamification and by mapping lesson formats from presentation-tool games and catalog-system games to physical, mixed-media, active, and live games. Because the source is a book review rather than an outcome study, it is best used as practitioner orientation rather than evidence of effectiveness."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, after the Cross et al. (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Šiptar (2023) also foregrounds learner adaptation as part of school-library game design: Pavey's handbook addresses students who are disengaged, afraid of failure, learning in a non-native language, dyspraxic, autistic, competitive, shy, or low in self-confidence, while also warning that device access, language availability, age, maturity, and local purchasing conditions shape which game formats are usable."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Build Partnerships and Community Pipelines`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Šiptar's (2023) review of Pavey also treats school-library games as partnership work: librarians need to know subject curricula, join curriculum planning where possible, collaborate with teachers, administrators, and parents, share materials with other schools, and present results clearly enough to secure support for future activities."

# **Al-Hafdi, F. S., & Alhalafawy, W. S. (2024). Ten years of gamification-based learning: A bibliometric analysis and systematic review. *International Journal of Interactive Mobile Technologies (iJIM), 18*(7), 188-212. https://doi.org/10.3991/ijim.v18i07.45335**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `37INN9W2`; Better BibTeX citation key `al-hafdiTenYearsGamificationBased2024`; attachment key `EK8L4RL3`.

Al-Hafdi and Alhalafawy present a two-part mapping of gamification-based learning in education: a bibliometric analysis of Web of Science records from 2012 to 2021 and a systematic review of 52 selected studies. Their purpose is to identify the dominant terms, countries, journals, authors, growth patterns, educational stages, game elements, and measured outcomes in gamification-based learning. The article treats gamification as the use of game elements and game design technology in non-game educational contexts, especially e-learning environments.

The bibliometric portion frames gamification as a rapidly expanding educational technology field. The authors report that the most common keywords include gamification, game-based learning, motivation, engagement, education, games, serious games, virtual reality, Moodle, Kahoot, social media platforms, students, e-learning, teaching, technology, and learning systems. They identify the United States, Spain, Britain, China, Germany, Australia, Italy, and Canada as major publication centers, with the United States leading by volume. They also name *Computers in Human Behavior* and *Computers & Education* as especially influential venues and identify Juho Hamari, Mitesh Patel, and Richard Landers as prominent authors in the mapped literature.

The article's growth finding is one of its clearest contributions. Within the search frame, gamification-based learning records increased from seven studies in 2012 to 917 studies in 2021. Al-Hafdi and Alhalafawy interpret this growth as evidence that gamification has become a vital and rapidly developing research area, especially because of its promise for performance, motivation, engagement, and active participation. Their discussion repeatedly connects gamification with educational theories such as goal-setting theory, self-efficacy theory, self-determination theory, social comparison theory, flow theory, and operant conditioning.

The systematic-review portion is more focused on instructional design. The authors report that university and primary education are the most common stages in the selected studies, while secondary and intermediate education appear less frequently. The game elements most often used are points, leaderboards, and badges, followed by rewards, levels, challenges, progress bars, goals, narration, selection, avatars or virtual characters, forums, time, stars, and other digital stimuli. The measured learning outcomes most often addressed are achievement, performance, engagement, and motivation, with satisfaction, attitudes, competence, stimulation, awareness, psychological well-being, readiness, decision-making, cognitive load, independence, and related outcomes appearing less often.

For the larger GBLS project, the article offers useful background vocabulary for distinguishing gamification from fuller game-based library services. It helps explain why points, badges, leaderboards, levels, feedback, progress tracking, rewards, competition, and challenge structures appear frequently in educational platform design. It also reinforces an important caution already present in the GBLS review: evidence about gamified e-learning can support general claims about motivation and engagement, but it should not be treated as direct evidence for library gaming programs, tabletop play, circulating game collections, TTRPG services, VR labs, game jams, or other library-specific service models.

The article is therefore adjacent rather than directly applicable. It does not study libraries, librarians, public-library programming, school-library instruction, academic-library information literacy, game collections, library game metadata, community gaming, virtual library worlds, or patrons' experiences with game-based library services. Its bibliometric dataset is education-wide, and its systematic review focuses on experimental or quasi-experimental educational gamification studies, not library practice. Because `review.md` already includes more directly relevant library sources on gamification, including Seay, Downie and Proulx, Maranchak and Maranchak, Capdarest-Arest et al., and Moid et al., adding this article would likely duplicate broad gamification background without strengthening the GBLS evidence base.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because the article is a broad education-focused bibliometric and systematic review of gamification-based learning, not a study of game-based library services.

# Khademi Zare, S., Varnaseri, A., & Bayati, N. (2024). Identifying the applications of gamification for audience attraction in public libraries. *Libri, 74*(2), 171-184. https://doi.org/10.1515/libri-2023-0123

Unique identifier: `khademizareIdentifyingApplicationsGamification2024`; Zotero item key `GA7REAB4`; attachment key `7VQXMTHM`.

Khademi Zare, Varnaseri, and Bayati (2024) investigate how gamification might help public libraries attract audiences, improve participation, and make services more appealing. The article is framed around public libraries in Tehran province and the broader problem of maintaining library visibility, relevance, and use in a digital media environment. The authors argue that public libraries already have valuable resources, spaces, librarians, educational programs, and cultural services, but still need strategies that make those resources visible and engaging to diverse publics.

Methodologically, the study uses a qualitative design. Data were collected through semi-structured interviews with gamification experts selected through purposive and snowball sampling. Participants needed at least two consecutive years of gamification-industry experience or comparable expert recognition. The sample grew from seven to thirteen participants to reach saturation. The authors analyzed transcripts through content/thematic analysis, following Braun and Clarke's six-stage process: familiarization, initial coding, theme searching, theme review, theme definition, and writing up. They combined interview analysis with research-literature review to identify applications of gamification for public libraries.

The findings are organized into sixteen main themes and fifty-four to fifty-six subthemes. The main applications include holding events and challenges, promoting reading habits, making library interaction spaces more attractive, improving book lending, making library visits more appealing, increasing awareness of activities and programs, improving user experience, improving educational activities, increasing public-library brand loyalty, encouraging participatory and volunteer activities, nurturing talents and skills, guiding audiences toward library goals, improving book searching, developing scientific/economic activities, encouraging timely return of borrowed resources, and conducting marketing activities. The article repeatedly frames gamification as a way to combine competition, cooperation, rewards, progress tracking, feedback, personalization, dashboards, missions, ranks, badges, and social interaction.

For GBLS, the strongest contribution is that the article treats audience attraction as a whole-library service problem rather than a single program type. Gamification is proposed for reading promotion, library tours, website and social-media engagement, membership processes, workshop participation, volunteer drives, peer mentoring, book searches, shelf exploration, returns, marketing, and even knowledge-production initiatives. This broad list helps the review show that game-based library service can be embedded in circulation, outreach, discovery, programming, user experience, and community participation, not only in stand-alone game nights.

The article is also useful because it recognizes cultural localization. Although the authors present play as a widely shared human activity, they note that Iranian public libraries serve linguistically and culturally diverse communities and that game elements should be adapted to local cultures, values, languages, and target participants. This supports a GBLS caution: points, badges, leaderboards, and rewards cannot simply be copied from commercial systems. They need to align with audience needs, local service goals, community expectations, and equitable access.

At the same time, the evidence is exploratory. The study identifies possible applications through expert interviews; it does not implement a gamified library system or measure changes in attendance, lending, reading behavior, user satisfaction, learning, retention, or volunteer participation. The authors themselves call for future comparative and controlled studies using gamified prototypes for different user groups, age segments, mechanics, point systems, narratives, and reward structures. For the GBLS review, this makes the source valuable as a design map and policy prompt, but not as proof that gamification increases public-library use.

The operational implication is that large-scale public-library gamification requires infrastructure. The authors recommend a central platform, application, website, or dedicated system that can support interactions, invitations, dashboards, progress bars, rewards, marketing, and community communication. That recommendation connects the article to GBLS sustainability concerns: gameful services can become attractive and integrated, but only if libraries plan the technology, staffing, privacy, local adaptation, assessment, and maintenance needed to keep them useful.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Maranchak and Maranchak (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Khademi Zare, Varnaseri, and Bayati (2024) extend public-library gamification from individual activities to audience attraction as a whole-library strategy. Through interviews with 13 gamification experts, they identify applications for events, reading habits, interactive spaces, lending, visits, program awareness, user experience, education, brand loyalty, volunteering, skill development, book searching, timely returns, marketing, and knowledge-production activities, showing how gameful design can be imagined across the public-library service ecology."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Start with Purpose, Audience, and Local Need`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Khademi Zare et al. (2024) make local audience fit a design requirement: even though play may be broadly recognizable, game elements in public libraries should be adapted to local cultures, languages, values, service goals, and target participants rather than imported as generic points, badges, and leaderboards."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Evidence Gaps`, after the Adetayo et al. (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Khademi Zare et al. (2024) add a public-library version of the same evidence gap: expert interviews can generate a rich map of possible gamification applications, but the field still needs implemented prototypes, comparison groups, age- or audience-specific testing, and measures of lending, attendance, reading, satisfaction, learning, volunteering, and retention."

# **Maluck, T. (2013). Tales from Play It Loud. *Library Trends, 61*(4), 779-789. https://doi.org/10.1353/lib.2013.0014**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `RT9RP42S`; Better BibTeX citation key `maluckTalesPlayIt2013`; attachment key `9S23S8BU`.

Maluck reports on introducing and managing the Play It Loud gaming program while serving as supervising young adult librarian at Richland Library Northeast in Columbia, South Carolina. The article is a practitioner case report rather than a formal experiment, but it is unusually concrete about program design, audience fit, facilitation, observed patron behavior, and how library gaming can be interpreted through youth-development goals. The program began from local evidence of demand: teens were already playing games in the building, gaming magazines circulated visibly, and the library system had Wii consoles, Wii games, and tabletop games available for branch programming.

The design choices are central to the article. Play It Loud was intentionally marketed as a loud, social program rather than a quiet library activity, and the branch's meeting room made that possible without disturbing other users. The room layout placed the Wii and projector in the center, with tabletop games along the sides. The library selected multiplayer Wii titles such as *New Super Mario Bros. Wii*, *Super Smash Bros. Brawl*, *Mario Kart*, *Wii Sports Resort*, *Just Dance*, and *Kirby's Return to Dreamland*, along with tabletop games such as chess, checkers, *Clue*, *Scrabble*, *Munchkin*, *Uno*, *Zombie Dice*, and *Jenga*. Maluck drew explicitly on Scott Nicholson's advice that library gaming should offer frequent participation opportunities and a mix of Strategy, Narrative, Action, Knowledge, and Social game archetypes.

Play It Loud was originally conceived as a teen program, but in practice it became a family and intergenerational program. It met monthly on Saturday afternoons, later twice monthly in the summer, and averaged twenty-one participants across the fiscal year, usually ages nine to sixteen with occasional younger children and adults. Patrons began bringing controllers from home, asking about the program when it was not scheduled, and treating it as part of the branch's weekend identity. Maluck also describes operational boundaries: no snacks near controllers, no M-rated or first-person shooter games, attention to safety during active Wii play, and later an age limit of ten and older because very young children could derail the teen-oriented experience.

A second program, Go Your Own Way, emerged from Play It Loud when the board game Go proved difficult to sustain beside the noise and immediacy of Wii play. With support from the American Go Foundation, the library obtained equipment, instructional materials, promotional materials, and related manga. Teen volunteers helped teach the game, and First Capture Go allowed new players to begin with short, low-frustration rounds before moving to larger boards and full rules. Attendance for the standalone Go program was smaller, often ten or fewer participants, but Maluck emphasizes the quality of interaction: parent-child play, cross-age learning, teen tutors, and a game structure that allowed different reasoning strengths to become visible.

The article's assessment frame comes from the Search Institute's 40 Developmental Assets for adolescents. Maluck maps observed behaviors from Play It Loud and Go Your Own Way onto assets such as family support, positive family communication, other adult relationships, caring neighborhood, community valuing youth, service to others, safety, family boundaries, positive peer influence, youth programs, honesty, responsibility, and cultural competence. Examples include parents learning games with children, teens setting up the room and teaching games, staff and security guards relating to youth positively, players enforcing fair play, youth being invited into other library programs, and teens moving from being unsupervised library users toward volunteering or joining the Teen Advisory Board.

For GBLS, Maluck is valuable because it treats public-library gaming as relational infrastructure rather than a simple entertainment draw. The case shows how multiplayer digital and analog games can create repeated contact between youth, parents, staff, volunteers, and peers; how facilitation practices such as turn-taking, handshake rituals, content selection, age boundaries, and room layout shape the social outcomes of play; and how a recreation-first program can become a bridge to advisory boards, volunteering, other library programs, and more explicitly educational activities. The article also cautions that one program cannot serve every age group equally well, that analog and digital games may require different acoustic and attentional environments, and that educational extensions may meet patron resistance if they feel bolted onto a beloved recreation program.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## Clubs and Recurring Play Groups`, after the Copeland et al. (2013) paragraph. Proposed text: "Maluck (2013) provides a public-library youth counterpart through Richland Library Northeast's Play It Loud program, a recurring Wii and tabletop gaming series that averaged twenty-one participants and became a weekend fixture. The case shows how recurring play groups depend on local demand signals, multiplayer-friendly game selection, room acoustics, regular scheduling, staff facilitation, teen volunteers, age boundaries, and links to adjacent programs such as Go instruction, anime club, volunteering, and Teen Advisory Board participation."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Brown and Kasper (2013) paragraph. Proposed text: "Maluck (2013) similarly argues that public-library gaming outcomes can be read through youth-development assets rather than attendance alone. His observations of Play It Loud and Go Your Own Way connect gaming to family communication, supportive nonparent adults, fair play, youth service, safety, responsibility, cultural competence, cross-gender participation, and pathways into other library relationships, while also showing the limits of anecdotal assessment and the need to separate entertainment value from educational extension."

# Intawong, K., Puritat, K., Leenaraj, B., & Arayaphan, W. (2023). A gamified mobile application for first-year student orientation to promote library services. *Journal of Librarianship and Information Science, 55*(1), 137-150. https://doi.org/10.1177/09610006211067273

Unique identifier: `leenarajGamifiedMobileApplication2023`; Zotero item key `6G4UFF6N`; attachment key `QKQJJU7W`.

Intawong, Puritat, Leenaraj, and Arayaphan (2023) test CMU Journey, a gamified mobile application for first-year student orientation at Chiang Mai University. The study addresses a familiar academic-library problem: first-year students need to learn library services, OPAC searching, call numbers, and physical library navigation, but traditional orientations and e-learning modules can feel passive, disconnected, or forgettable. The authors propose mobile gamification as a way to make orientation more exploratory, memorable, and motivating.

CMU Journey adapts a Pokémon Go-style loop to the university environment. Students use mobile devices and geolocation to explore important campus places, capture monsters, answer place-based questions, and collect rewards. For the library portion, the game focuses on the main library. The core loop is explore the important place, capture the monster, answer the monster's question, and collect the reward. The mechanics include score points, a leaderboard, inventory, choice, time pressure, and map navigation. Questions require students to investigate the physical environment, so the app links digital play to library space rather than replacing the library with a standalone quiz.
